_  That  the  ,’ wntiff, R. R. Grovey,  is a natural  born  citizen

years,  next  preceding  the  filing of this  petition.

That  the‘plaintiff,  R. R.'Grovey,.is  forty-four  (44) years
of age,  is  a member  of the  Negro  race,  has resided in  voting
precinct  No.  85-in- Harris  County,  Texas,  for  a  period  of
four  (4)  years next  preceding  the  holding  of the  statutory
‘democratic  primary  election  involved  in  this  case;.  that
plaintiff  paid his  poll tax  for the  year  1933  on the  31st  day
of  January,  A.  D.  1934  as  requited  by  the laws  of  the
state  of Texas, and holds  his official  receipt  No. 40712  there-
for; that  plaintiffAs  and jwas on July  9, 1934,  a duly quali-
nited States  and  the

constitution  and  laws  of the  State  of Texas;  that  plaintiff

That  Article  3101  and Article  3102  of the  Revised  Civil

Statutes  of Texas  provide  as  follows:

-

Art, 3101.  (3084)  Nominated  at Primary
On  primary  election  day  in  1926,  and  every  two  years.
_  thereafter,  candidates  for  Governor  and  for  all other  State
offices  to-be  chosen by vote  of the  entire  State,  and candi-
dates  for  Congress  end  all  district  officés  to  be chosen  by
the  vete  of any  district  cofhprising  more  than  one  county,
to be nominated  by each  organized  political  party  that  cast.
_  one  hundred  thousand  votes  or  more  at  the  las®  general
-  eléction,  shall,  together.  with all candidates  for  offices  to  be °
filled  by the voters  of a county,  or of a portion  of a county,
be  nominated  in  primary  elections  by the  qualified  voters
of such party.  «(Acts Ist C. 8. 1905, p. 549.)

-

Art. 3102.  (3086)  Date of Primary
The fourth Saturday  in Jul uly 1996, and  every  two  years
thereafter  shall  be  general  primary  election  day,  and  pri-
_  mary  elections  to  nominate  candidates  fora  general  elec-
tion  shall  be  held'on  another  day,  except  when  specially
authorized.
No. person  shall  be  declared  the  nominee  of  .
any political  party  at  any  primary  election  for  any  State
or or District office  unless  he has  complied  with  every  require-
ment  of all laws  applicable  to primary  ang  other  elections,
[fol.  5} and  has  received  a  majority  of all  the  votes  cast
at  such  primary  elections  for  all candidates  for  such  office.
If at  the  general  primary  election  for  any  political  party,
no  candidate  becomes  the nominee  for  any  State  or  district
office  under  this  article,  a  second  primary  election  shall  be
-  held  by such  politieal  party,  in  the  State,  or  such  district,
or  districts,  as  the  ease  may  be, on  the fourth  Saturday
August  succeeding  such general primary  election,  and
the  name  of .the  two. candidates  who  receive  the  highest
number  of  votes  for  any office  for  which  nomination  was
made  at  the general  election  shall be placed  on the official  ©
ballot  as  candidates  for such  office  at such  second  primary.
The second primary election  shall be conducted
the law prescribed  for conducting  the general  primary

be any  challenge  of sfich  vote,  legal  cause  for  same  shall  be  .
heard  arid decided  according  to the law provided  in the  case
of such  challenge.  —
In case  no  challenge  is made,  such  poll  tax  receipt,  after
same  is marked  ‘‘voted”’  as  provided  by law, shall  be mailed
back  to the said  County  Clerk.  But  in the case  of challenge,
such poll tax
receipt together  with  affidavits  relating  thereto
shall  be maild by said  Judge  of election  of  (to)  the County
Clerk  of  such  County  who  shall  keep  same  for  thirty  (30)
days,  and  if no  demand  be made  for  the  production  of same
before  ayybody  or  persons  in  authority  within  said  time,
said  County  Clerk  shall  deliver  such receipt  to  the  owners
thereof.  When  voted,  the  Judge  of  election  shall  mark
opposite  the  name  of  such Absentee  voter  the  word  ‘‘ Ab-
sentee.’’

‘That  on  or  about  the  9th  day  of  July,  A.  D.,  1934 the
plaintiff,  R.  R.  Grovey,  was  a  qualified  elector  as  defined
by the  laws  of Texas, and  expected  to be  absent  from  Har-
ris  County,  Texas,  on  July  28, A.  D.  1934,  and  that  he  did
on  said date,  which  was  not  more  than  twenty days  nor  less
than  three  days  prior  to  said  July  28,  1934  make  his  per-
sonal  appearance  before  the  defendant,  Albert  Townsend,
Clerk  of  said  County  of  Harris,  State  of  Texas,  as  afore-
said,  and  then  and  there  delivered  to said  defendant,  Albert
-  Townsend,  County  Clerk  of Harris  County,  Texas,  his  Said
poll  tax  receipt  mentioned  above,  and  then  and  there  re-
'  quested  said  defendant,  Albert  Townsend,  County  Clerk  of  ©
the Harris  County,  Texas,  to deliver
to this plaintiff one bal-  .
lot  which  had  been  prepared  in  accordante  with  law  for
use  in  the  statutory  Democratic  primary  election,  held  on
-  (fol. 11]  July  28, 1984,  in order  that  plaintiff  might  cast  an
absentee  ballot in  said election  as  provided  by said  Article
2956  of  the  Revised  Civil  Statutes  of Texas;  that  the  shid
-Albert  Townsend  was  on  the  said  date  when  this  plaintiff
appeared before  him  as  aforesaid,  conducting  absentee  vot-
ing for  said  statutory  Democratic  primary  election  held  on
July  28,  1934,  in  accordance  with  the  provisions  of  said
Article  2956  of .the  Revised  Civil  Statutes  of  the  State  of
¥

.  Texas.

That  the  defendant,  Albert  Townsend,  although  well
knowing  all  of the facts  and  matters  set  out  above,  did  re-
fuse  on  said  dafe  to deliver  to this  plaintiff  one  ballot  which
had  been  prepared  in  accordance  with  the  law  for  use  in

.
=~

*

7
said  statutory  Democratic  primary  election  held  on  July
28, 1934, and that the said defendant; Albert  Townsend,  is a
statutory  officer  in  charge  of  absentee  voting  in  the  said
election,  and  that  the  said  Albert  Townsend  did  refuse  to
permit  this  plaintiff  to have,  or  to  mark  such  ballot,  or  to
vote,  or  to  cast  an  absentee  ballot  in  said  election  as  au-
thorized  and required  by said Article  2956  of the  Revised.
Civil  Statutes  of Texas ; that  the  action  of the  said  defend-
ant,  Albert  Townsend,  in  refusing  to deliver  to  this  plain-
tiff  one  such  ballot,  and  in refusing  to permit  this  plaintiff
to  receive  or  to  mark  one  such  ballot  and  in  refusing  and
preventing  and  depriving  this plaintiff  of his  right  to  vote
.  and  cast  an  absentee  ballot  in ‘said  statutory  Democratic
primary  election  held  on  J uly 28, 1934,  was  willful,  wrong-
ful,  unlawful,  and  in violation  of the  rights  of plaintiff  as
hereinafter  set  forth,-and  that  by  reason  of  such  wreng-
[fol.12]  fully,  willful  and  unlawful  acts  of  the  defendant,
Albert  Townsend,  this  plaintiff  has  been  damaged  in  the
sum  of ten  dollars  ($10.00),  and  said-acts  of defendant,  Al-
bert  Townsend,  violated  plaintiff’s  rights  under  the  14th
and  15th  Amendments  to the  Constitution  of  the  United
States.  -
VI
That  the  defendant,  Albert  Townsend,  at  the  time-  he
refused  to permit  this  plaintiff  to cast  said  absentee  ballot,  —
and  prevented  the plaintiff  from  castg  said  absentee  bal-
lot, on  or  about  the 9th day of July, A. D. 1934, did then  and
there  give to this  plaintiff  as  his reason  and justification  for
such  denial  of plaintiff’s  iegal  right to vote  in said  election,
the  following  matters  and  things,  to-wit:
A.  certain  purported  resolution,  passed  and
adopted  by a  certain  commission  or  administrative  board
or  agency  of government  or  officials  of the  State  of Texas,
created  under  and~by
Article  3167  of  the  Revised  Civil
Statutes  of Texas,
known  as  the  State Democratic  Con-
vention  of Texas.  Said  resolution  was  adopted  on the  24th
day of May,  1932,  and  reads  as  follows  :

First.

:

|

:

|

**Be  it  resolved,  that  all  white  citizens  of  the  State  of
Texas  who  are  qualified  to  vote  under  the  Constitution
and the  laws-of  the  state  shall  be eligible  to membership  in
the Democratic  party  and  as  such  entitled  to participate  in
its deliberations,”’

‘

\ Second.  Certain  instructions,  which  the  said  defendant,
APert  Townsend,  claimed  he  had received  from  a  certain
[fol.13]
state  commission  or  administrative  board  or
agency  of government  or  eiicials  of  the  State  of  Texas,
created  by  Article  3118  of  the  Revised  Civil  Statutes  of
Texas  and  known!  as  the  County  Democratic  Executive
Committee  of Harris  County,  Texas,  by which  instructions,
said  defendant,  Albert  Townsend,  advised  this  plaintiff
that  he had  been  instructed  and  advised  that  Negro  voters
and  electors  otherwise  qualified  had  no legal  right  to  vote  —
or  cast  abseritee  ballots  in  said election,  solely  and  only
on  account  of  their  race  and  colot  as  Negroes,  and  that
since  plaintiff  was  a  member  of  the  Negro race,  said  de-
fendant,  Albert  Townsend,  advised  this  plaintiff  that  solely
and only because  he  was  a  Negro,  he  could  not  deliver  to
plaintiff  an  absentee  ballot  and  was  compelled  to  prevent  —
plaintiff  from ‘casting  an  absentee  ballot,  or  otherwise
~
voting  an  absentee  vote  in  said  statutory  Democratic  pri-
mary  election  held  on July 28, 1934,

That  said  purported  reasons  and  justifications  were  and.
are  no  legal  excuse  for  said  wrongful  and unlawful acts  of
the  deféndant in  depriving  this  plaintiff  of his  legal  right
+o cast  an  absentee  ballot in  said statutory  Democratic  pri-

‘

-

bh

*.

‘mary  elections, and

and that said wrongful-and  unlawful  acts —

>of  the defendant,  Albert  Townsend,  as  well.as,  the  said
reason  and  justification  for  said  acts  constitute  a  violation
of  plaintiff’s  rights  under  the  14th  and  15th  Amendments
to  the  Constitution  of the United  States  in  that  delegates
of  the  power  of  the  State  of  Texas  have  discharged  their
official  functions  in  such  a  way  as  to  discriminate  against
[fol.  14]  plaintiff  on  account  of  his  race  and  color  alone,
and  thereby  have  denied  to  this  plaintiff  the  due  process
‘  of law  and  the equal  protection  of laws  guaranteed  to  this
plaintiff  by the  said  14th  Amendment  to  the  Constitution
of  the  United  States.
The  said  delegates  of  the  state’s
power  have  also used  their  said  official  functions in  such  a
way  as  to  abridge  the  right  of  plaintiff  to  vote,  which  is
guaranteed  to him  against  abridgment  by the  15th  Amend-
ment  to the  Constitution  of the  United  States.

VII

_

That  the above  quoted  resolution  of the  State  Democratic
Convention  of Texas  and  the  above  mentioned  instructions

‘

.

sae

,

9
given to the defendant  by the County Democratic  Executive  ~
Committee  of Harris  County,  Texas,  are  further  void  and
no  legal  defense  or  justification  for  the  wrongful  and  un-
lawful  acts  of  defendant  in  depriving  this plaintiff  of  his
legal  right  to  vote,  forthe  following  reasons,  td-wit:
That  Article  3107  of the Revised  Civil  Statutes  of Texas
as amended  now  provides  as  follows:  -

‘Every  political  party  in this  State  through  its  State
Executive  Committee  shall  have  the  power  to prescribe  the
qualifications  of its  own  members  and  shall  in its  own  way
‘  determine  who  shall  be qualified  to  vote  or  otherwise  par-
ticipate  in  such  political  party;  provided  that  no  person.
shall  ever  ‘be denied  the  right-to  participate  in a primary
in this  State  because  of former political  views or affiliations  .
or  because  of membership  or -non-membership  in organiza-
tions  other  than  the political  party.’*  (Acts  1927, 40th  Leg.  -
Ist C. S. p. 193, ch.  67, § 1.)
j

‘

{fol.15]

That  the  Supreme  Court  of Texas  has  held  in the
_  case  of Love  v.  Wilcox,  119  Texas  256,  28 S; ‘W.  (2nd) 515.
'  that  the  said  article  3107  is  valid  and  constitutes  a  pro-
_  hibition  agajnst making former political  affiliations  or mem-  -
bership  in  organizations  other  than  the  political  party  a
basis  for  excluding  otherwise  qualified  voters  from  par-
ticipating  in primary  elections  in the  State  of Texas.  That  -
by upholding  the  validity  of said  Article  3107 ,  the  State  of.
Texas  has  destroyed  the  untrammeled  power  of  the  man-  |
agers  of statutory  primary  elections  in  Texas  to  fix  the
qualifications  for  voting  or  other: participation  therein  and
has destroyed  the one  historical  test  of party  regularity  and
membership,  namely  the  support  of party  nominees  in past
elections.

ben

But  that  the  State  of  Texas,  through  a  decision  of  the
Supreme  Court  of  Texas,  styled  Bell  et  al.  v.  Hill  et  al.,
motion  number  11520,  decided  July  20,  1934,  and  not  yet.
officially  reported,  has  upheld  the  validity  of  the  above
_  quoted  resolution  of  the  State  Democratic  Convention  of
Texas,  and  the  above  mentioned  instructions  of the  Count
Democratic  Executive  Committee  of Texas  as  a valid  exer-
cise of the  alleged  inherent  power  of the  managers  of pri-
mary  elections  in Texas  to make  race  and color  a  basis  of
excluding  otherwise  qualified  voters  from  participation  in
such  elections.
.That  by  setting  up  a  discrimination  in

10

granting  its  protection  between  ‘dcnitiene  of  former
political  affiliations  and  membership ia  other  organizations
fhan the political  party  and the classification  of  race  and
color,  the  State  of  Texas  has  denied  to  this  plaintiff  and
[fol. 16]  to  all other  qualified  Negro  voters  the equal  pro«
tection  of the laws  and  the  due  process-of  laws,  which  are
, Zuaranteed  by the  14th  Amendment  to,the  Constitution  of
the  United  States, ‘and  has  abridged
the  right  of  plaintiff.
and  all  other  qualified  Negro  voters  to\ vote, in violation  of
the  provisions  of  the  15th  Amendment  to  the Constitution
of the  United  States.

‘That  in  the said  case of Bell et al. v.  Hill et al.,  the Su-
preme  Court  of-Texas  did  hold  that  the  right  of. the man-
agers  of statutory  primary  elections  in  Texas  to exclude  -
plaintiff  and  other  qualified  Negro  voters  from voting  in
such  election  was  guaranteed  and  protected to  such  party
managers  by Sections  2 and  27  of  the  Bill  of  Rights  (Ar-
_.  ticle  1)  of  the  Constitution  of  the  State  of Texas  which

read  as  follows:

co  «

‘*Sec.  2. All  political  power  is  inherent  in  the  people,
and  all  free  governments  are  founded  on  their  authority,
and  instituted  for  their  benefit.  The  faith  of the  people  of
Texas  stands  pledged  to  the  preservation  of a  Republican
form  of government,  and,  subject  to  this  limitation  only,
they  have  at all times  the  inalienable right  to alter,  reform
or  abolish  their  government  in  such  manner  as  they  may
think  expedient.’’

‘*Sec.  27.  The  citizens  shall  have  the  right,  in  a  peace-
able manner,  to assemble  together  for  their  common  good;  -
and  apply  to  those  invested  with  the  powers  of  govern-
ment  for  redress  of grievances  or  other  purposes,  by peti-
tion,  address  or  remonstrance.’’

That as so construed,  said section-  2 and 27 of the Bill of
Rights  of Texas  are  void as  denying  to the classification  of
{fol.  17]  race  and  color  the protection  which  is guaranteed
by said  Article  3107  of the Revised  Civil  Statutes  of Texas,
to  the  classifications  of past  party  affiliations  and  member-
ship  or  non-membership  in  organizations  other  than  the
political  party.

That by reason of all of  the above, plaintiff has been
denied  the  equal protection  of the laws,  and  the  due  process
;  of  law guaranteed  to him by the 14th Amendment  to  the

.

;

11
Constitution  of  the  United  States,  and  plaintiff’s  right. to
vote  guaranteed to him  by,the  15th  Amendment  to the Con-
stitution  of the  United  States,  has  been-abridged,  and  that  ~
plaintiff’s  said  consfitutional  riglits  have  been  denied  .to
him  by the  State  of Texas  and  by agencies  vested with  the
State’s  power.
i
Plaintiff  further  alleges  that by a  consistent  course  of
statutory  enactment  and  judicial  decisions,  since  the  origi-
nal  passage  of.  the  statutory  primary  election  laws  of
_  Texas  in  1903,  the  State  of  Texag  has  deprived  the man-
agers  of such  elections  of all  power  to fix qualifications  for
voting  therein,  inconsistent  with  the  qualifications  for
electors  provided  in the  constitution  and  laws  of the  State
of Texas,  except.in  the  one  instance  where  the party  man-  .
agers  have  been  authorized  and  permitted  and  protected’
by said  course  of  legislative  enactment  and  judigfal .  deci-
sions  in  excluding  Negroes  from  voting  in  said  statutory
primary  elections.
Aa
|

7
Vu
That  the  Democratic  party  is  a  national  organization,
and  that  the  Democratic  party  has: not  passed  any  rule  or
resolution  or  order  excluding  Negroes,  and  has  not  au-
[fol. 18) ‘thorized  any  of  its  agencies  or  branches  to pass
“any  resolution,  rule,  or  order  excluding  Negroes,  or  for-
bidding  Negroes  from  .becoming  members  of  the  Demo-
cratic  party  and that  no state  convention  of the Democratic  ._
party  has the  power  by virtue  of its being, to pass  any  such
rule,  resolution,  or  order,  and  that  the  Democratic  party
has  never  conferred  power  upon  the State  Democratic  con-
vention  of Texas  to pass  any  such  resolution,  rule, or  order.

;

-

IX
That  in said  statutory  Democratic  primary election: held
on  July 28, 1934, nominations  were  made  for United  States
Senator,  United  States  Congressman,  Governor  of  the
State  of Texas,  senators  and  representatives  to the legisla-
ture  of  the  State  of  Texas  and  other  State  and  County
officers, and that it  is a  matter of common  and general
knowledge  in  Texas  that  such  nominations  are  equivalent
to elections  to such  office  and  that  by reason  thereof,  which
is a matter  of general  and  common  knowledge,  since  plain-

.  tiff  did  not  get  to  vote.in  said  election  on  July  28th,  1934,
plaintiff  has  been  fully  and  effectively  disfranchised  by

legal right to vote in  said  election.

-

,  a

That  the  defendant,  Albert  Townsend,  is a  State  officer

‘That  the  Democratic  State  Convention of Texas  and the

De  BS court  erred  in  holding  that  the  State  of

[fol. 30)  trial  herein.

On this  Sept.  25th  day  of  September,  A.  D.  1934,  at a
regular term  of this court,  came  on  to be heard in  this  cause

:

:

19

the  motion  of R.  R.  Grovey,  plaintiff,  to set  aside  the  judg-
ment  of the  court  rendered  against  him in  favor  of the  de-
fendant,  Albert  Townsend,  on  the  24th  day  September,
A.  D.  1934, in  the  above  entitled  and  numbered  cause,  and
_
to  grant  plaintiff  a  new  trial  herein,  and  said  motion  hav-
'  ing been  duly  heard  and  considered  by the  court,  the  court
is of the  opinion  and  finds  that  said  motion  for.a  newtrial
should  be overruled.

It is  therefore  considered,  ordered,  and  adjudged  by the
court  that  said  motion  for  a  new  trial  herein  be,  and  the
same  is hereby  overruled,  to  which  said  judgment and  rul-
ing  of  the  court,  plaintiff  then  and  there  excepted,  and
plaintiff’s  exceptions  were,  and  are  allowed.

SUPREME  COURT  OF THE  UNITED  STATES
OCTOBER  TERM,  1934

ss

No. 563

R. R. GROVEY,
|

v8.
-ALBERT  TOWNSEND.

:

Petitioner,

“e

PETITION  FOR WRIT  OF CERTIORARI  TO JUSTICE
"COURT,  PREOINOT  No,  1, POSITION  No. 2, HARRIS
“  OOUNTY,  TEXAS.

To  the  Honorable  the Chief Justice  and  Associate  Justices

of the  Supreme  Court  of the Dnited  States:

The  petitioner,  R. R. Grovey, hereby respectfully applies
for  a  Writ  of  Certiorari,  whereby  the  Justice  Court,  Pre-
cinct  No.  1, Position  No.  2, in and  for  the  County  of Harris
and State  of Texas, will  be required  to certify  to this  Honor-
able  Court  for  its  review  the  transcript  of  record  in  the  |
case  entitled  ‘‘R.  R. Grovey,  plaintiff,  vs.  Albert  Townsend,
defendant,  No.  63,915,’’  in which  the  said Justice  Court,  on
September  24,  1934,  sustained  the  general  demurrer  of re-
spondent  to  petitioner’s  petition,  dismissed  petitioner’s
suit,  and  rendered  judgment  in  favor  of  respondent,  and
in  which  said  Justice  Court  on  September  25,  1934,  over-

lp

2
ruled  the petitioner’s  motion  for.a new  trial;  petitioner’s
petition and action being to recover  damages  from  respond-
ent for his wrongful  refusal  to permit  petitioner  to cast  an
absentee  vote  in  a  statutory  Democratic  primary  election,
for  which  respondent  was  the  statutory  official  in  charge
of absentee  voting.  In  support  hereof,  under  Section  344
(b),  Title  28,  U.  S.  Code,  Judicial  Code,  Section  237  (b),
your  petitioner  respectfully  alleges:
‘First.  This  is the  fourth  time  that  a  Negro  citizen  has
found  it necessary  to apply to this Honorable  Court  for the
protection  of his civil  right to vote  in the statutory  Demo-
cratic primary  elections  of Texas.

:

(a)  In  Love  v.  Griffith  (decided  October  20, 1924),  266
U. S. 32, the question was  moot before  it reached  this  Court,
but  this  Court  said  that  ‘‘If  the case stood  here  as  it stood
before  the  court  of first  ingtance  it would  present  a  grave
question  of  constitutional  law  and  we  should  be  astute  to
avoid hindrances  in the way  of taking it up.”’
(b)  In Nixon  ¥. Herndon  (decided  March  7, 1927),  273
U.  S. 536, the  question  was  not  moot  and‘the  Negro’s  con-
stitutionally  protected  civil right  to  vote  in the  statutory
Democratic  primary  elections  of Texas  was  established  be-
yond  doubt  and  a Texas  statute  which  sought  to  destroy
the  right  was  declared  unconstitutional,  this  Court  saying
that  ‘‘it  is too  clear  for  extended  argument  that  color  can-
not be made the basis of a  statutory  classification  affecting
the  right set up in this case.”’  (Italics  ours.)

(c) Nizon  v.  Condon  (decided  May 2, 1932),  286 U. 8. 73,
arose  when,  taking  refuge  behind  the  rule  that  the  Four-
teenth  and  Fifteenth  Amendments  are  restraints  upon  the
States  and  not  upon  private  individuals,  another  Texas
statute passed as was said because of ‘‘an emergency and
imperative  public  necessity’’  created  by Nizon  v.  Herndon,
supra,  ‘lodged  the’ power”’  (as  this  Court found  in  Nizon

3
v.  Condon,  supra)  in the State  Democratic  Executive  Com-
mittee  of  Texas  to  deprive  Negroes  of  their  civil  right  to.
vote in thé Texas  statutory  Democratic  primary  elections.
This  Court  held  that  the State  Committee was an  organ  “‘of
the  State  itself’’  and  laid  down  the broad  rule  that  (p. 88)
‘*Whether,  in  given  circumstances,  parties  or  their  .com-
mittees  are  agencies  of government  within  the  Fourteenth  -
er  the Fifteenth  Amendment is a question  which  this  Court
will  determine  for  itself.  It is not  concluded  upon  such  an
inquiry  by decisions  rendered  elsewhere.’’

(d) Following  the  second Nizon case,  Negroes were  again  —

deprived  of their  civil  right  to vote  in the  statutory  Demo-
cratic  primary  elections  of. Texas,  and  the instant  case  pre-
sents  the  fourth  challenge  to  the constitutionality  of  that
deprivation,  under  the  Fourteenth  and  Fifteenth  Amend-
ments  to the  Constitution  of the United  States.

Second.  In  Nixon  v.  Condon,  supra,  as  proof.that  the
State  Democratic  Exeeutive  Committee  of  Texas  had  no
power  by virtue  of its  being  to say  what  citizens  could  be-.
come  Democrats  and  what  citizens  could  not,  this  Court
made  the  following  statement  at  page  84:  ‘“Whatever  in-
herent  power  a  State  political  party  has  to  determine  the
content  of its  membership  resides  in the  State  convention.
Bryce,  Modern  Democracies,  Vol.  2, p. 40."’  (Italics  ours.)
Assuming  that  the  Democratic  Party  is a ‘‘state  political
party’’—which  petitioner  alleges  that  it is not—and  plac-
ing a  favorable  significance  upon  the  word  ‘‘Whatever”’  in
the  above  quotation  from  this  Court,  the  State  Democratic
Convention  of Texas  promptly  passed,  on  May 24, 1932,  the
following  resolution  (R. 7) :

,

:

**Be  it resolved,  that  all  white  citizens  of  the  State
of Texas  who  are  qualified  to vote  under  the  Constitu-

4

tion  and  Laws  of the  State  shall  be eligible  to member-
ship  in  the  Democratic  party  and  as  such  eligible  for
participation  in  its  deliberations’’  (the  primaries).
See  Bell  v.  Hill, 74 8. W.  (2d)  113,  and  Mason  v.  Com-
mittee,  74  S.  W.  (2d)  326,  where  this.  difference  in
reading  of resolution  may  be found.

.

ale  CS  ee  ee  ee  oe
in Instant  Case.
lenged

Third.  Since  its  adoption  and  tne  deprivation  under  its
authority  of  the  legal  right  of  Negroes  to  vote in  the
statutory  Dernocratic  primary  elections  of Texas,  the  above  —
resolution  has  been  before  two  Texas  Courts  of  Civil  Ap-
‘peals  and  the  Supreme  Court  of.  Texas.
County  Demo-
cratic  Executive  Committee  v.  Booker.  53  8. W.  (2d)  123;
Bell v.  Hill, 74 S. W.  (2d)  113;  and  Mason  v.  County Demo-
cratic  Executive  Committee,  74  S.  W.  (2d)  326.
These
cases  are  tacitly  relied  upon  by the court  below  as  author-
ity for  the  judgment  in the  instant  case.

;

-

(a)  In the  Booker  Case  (Cobbs,  J., dissenting)  the  Court
of Civil  Appeals  at  San  Antonio,  through  Special  Justice
Searcy,  thought  that  its  decision  upholding  the  resolution
was  ‘‘in  line with  the  opinion  of the  Supreme  Court  of the
.  United  States  in Nixon  v.  Congon.’’  Expressing  the  same
thought  the late  Chief  Justice  €ly, in a concurring  opinion,
said  (p. 129) that  Nixon  v.  Condon,  ‘“‘after  destroying  the
ship  of party power  and rule,  threw  out  a  spar  upon  which
the  party  might  keep  its head  above  the  tummultous  political  |
sea  of  1932,  that  spar  being  the  convention  of  the  party,
not  acting  on  any  State  mandate,  but  on  its  unfettered  .

_  volition.’’  But  this  spar  was  discovertd  only  after  the
Chief  Justice  had  thus  written  (p.  129):  ‘‘However  much
the  logic  and  strength  of  the  minority  opinion.  in  the  case
of  Nixon  v.  Condon,  herein  cited,  may  excel  those  of  the
majority  opinion,  however  much  we  may  differ  with  the

=)

5

niajority  opinion,  representing  only  five  of the  nine  judges
of  the  Federal Supreme  Court,  still  it  is  the  law  of ‘the
land,  and  this  Court  will  endeavor to  follow  not  only  its

*  -spirit  but  its letter.’’

(b)  The  Mason  case  merely followed  the  Booker  and Bell

cases.

;

(ce) In the  Bell  case,  decided  July 20,  1934,  the  Supreme
-
Court  of  Texas  gave  its  approval  to  the  Booker  case,  but
expressly  disagreed  with  this  Court’s  decision in  Nizon v.
Condon,  supra,  saying through  Chief  Justice  Cureton  (p.
121):
_*  “Justice  Cardozo  held  that  a  proper  construction
of this  statute  was  that  the  power  to  declare  the  mem-
bership  of the  party  had  been  conferred  upon  the  State
Committee  by law,  and  that  the  effect  of  this  was  to
make  the  committee  a  state agency,  which  could  not
discriminate  between  the  white  and  N egro races  in fix-
ing  the  membership  of  the Democratic  Party.  With
this  construction  of this  Texas  statute  we  cannot  agree.
The  State  Executive  Committee  of  the  Democratic
Party  in  Texas is  just  what  its  name  implies—a  com-
mittee  clothed  with  executive  power  to  carry  out  the  —
(Italies ours.)
will  of the  party.’’

 @

-The  Supreme  Court  of  Texas further  held  that,  if this
Court’s  construction  of  Article  3107  of  the  Revised  Civil  .
Statutes  of  Texas  should  prevail  upon  the  proposition  of
whether  the  State  Democratic  Executive  Committee  was  an
agency  of Government  within the 14th  or  15th  Amendment
(which  this  Court  said  in  Niwon  v.  Condon  it ‘‘will  deter-
mine  for  itself’’),  then  said  statute  would  be ‘‘plainly  void,
because  in  violation  of  sections  27  and  2  of  the  Bill  of
Rights’’  (Article  1) of the  Texas  naaartatene which  read
as  follows  (R. 10):

**Sec.  2.  All  political  power  is  inherent in  the  peo-
ple, and  all  free  governments  are  founded  on  their

6

authority,  and  instituted  for  their  benefit.  The  faith
~  of the  people  of Texas  stands  pledged  to the  preserva-
tion  of  a  Republican  form  of  government,  and,  sub-
'
ject  to  this  limitation  only,  they have  at  all  times  the
inalienable  right  to alter,  reform  or  abolish  their  gov-
ernment  in such  manner  as  they may  think  expedient.”’
**Sec.  27.  The  citizens  shall  have  the  right,  in  a
peaceable  manner,  to assemble  together  for  their  com-
mon  good;  and  apply  to  those  invested  with  the  pow-
ers  of  government  for  redress\of  grievances  or  other
purposes,  by petition,  address  or  temonstrance.’’

The  Supreme  Court  of Texas  further  keld  (p. 119)  tliat
the  power  thus  to  discriminate  against  Negroes  was  guar-
anteed  and  protected  by  ‘‘the  first  section  of  the  Bill  of
Rights,  the  First  Amendment  to ‘the  Constitution  of .the
United States.’’

Petitioner  challenges  said  Sections 2 and  27  of Article  1
(BilP of Rights  of the Constitution  of Texas,  fhus  construed,
and  the  decisions  construing  them,  as  violating  petitioner’s
rights  under  the  Fourteenth  and  Fifteenth  Amendments  to
the  Constitution  of the United  States  (R. 10).

_

Petitioner's  Injury.

Fourth.  On  July  28,  1934, a  statutory  Democratic  pri-  —

mary  election  was  held  in-the  State  of Texas,  of the  same
character  as  the  ones  involved  in  Nixon  v.  Herndon  and
Niwon  v.  Condon,  supra,  at  which  nominations,  equivalent
to  election,  were  made  for United  States  Senator,  United
.  States  Congressman,  and  state,  district,  county  and  pre-
cinct  officers.

In obedience to the mandate  of Article 2956 of  the’ Re-

vised  Civil  Statutes  of Texas,  absentee’  voting  wes  being
condutted  on July 9, 1934, by the respondent,  Albert  Town-
send, Who was the duly elected, qualified, and acting county
clerk  of Harris  County,  Texas.  Said article  2956 is quoted

7
in full in petitioner’s  original  petition  (B. 4),  the pith of
which  reads:

,

“‘Art.  2956.  Absentee  Voting.  Any  qualified elec-
tor, as defined  by the laws  of this  State,  who  expects  to
be  absent  from  the  County  of  his  or  her  residence  on  .
the  day  of  election  may  vote  subject  to  the  following
conditions,  towit:  At  some  time,  not  more  than  twenty~
(20)  days  nor  less  than  three  (3)  days  prior  to  the
.  date  of  such  election,-such  elector  shail  make  his  or
her personal  appearance  before  the Clerk  of the  County
of his  or  her  residence  and  shall  deliver-to  such  Clerk
his  or  her  poll  tax  receipt  or  exemption  certificate  en-
titling  him  or  her  to vote  at  such  election,  and  said
Clerk  shall  deliver  to such  elector  one  ballot  which  has
been  prepared  in  accordance  with  the  law  for  use  in
such  election  ...
The  provisions  of this  article,shall
apply  to  all  elections,  including  General,  Special,  and
Primary  elections.

5.

~

'

’

The petitioner  was on  said July 9, 1934, a ‘qualified  elec.
tor,  as  defined  by the  laws  of”’  Texas;  was  a  member  of
and  believer  in  the  tenets  of  the  Democratic  Party;  ex-
pected  to  be  absent  from  his  county  on  said  election  day;
made  his  personal  appearance  before  the  respondent  and
delivered  to  respondent  petitioner’s  poll  tax  receipt  en-
titling  petitioner  to  vote  in  the  election  involved  in  this
case;  but  respondent  refused to deliver  to petitioner  a  bal-
lot or  to permit  petitioner  to vote  in said  election,  respond-
ent  giving  as  his  sole  reason  and  justification  the  fact  that
petitioner  was  a  Negro,  and  that  under  the  above  quoted
resolution  of  the  State  Democratic  Convention  of  Texas
and  under  instructions  from  the County  Democratic  Exe-
cutive  Committee  of Harris  County,  Texas,  petitioner  was
ineligible  to  vote  in  said  election,  solely  and  only  because
petitioner  was  a member  of the  Negro  race.

Instant  Suit Brought.

Fifth.  Feeling  himself  aggrieved  and  damaged  by  his
said  disenfranchisement  in  the  sum  of  Ten  Dollars  (ten
times  the  damages  awarded  by the  jury  in Nixon'v.  Hern-
don, supra;  ten  times  the  amount  agreed  as  the measure  of  -
‘damages  by  the  parties  in  Nixon  v.  Condon,  supra;  and
twice  the  amount  of  damages  awarded  upon a trial  to  the
_  United  States  District  Judge  without  a  jury  in  Nizon  v
McCann,  No.  1660  Law,  U.S.  District  Court,  Western  Dis-
trict  of  Texas,  decided  February  7, 1934,  but  not  officially  ©
reported;  twice  the  amount  of  damages  awarded  by  the
jury  in Bliley  v.  West,  42 F.  (2d)  101;  (see  certified  copies
of  judgments  in Nicon  cases  printed  herewith  as  Appen-
dices  1,  2,  and  3),  petitioner,  on  September  10,  1934,
brought  suit  for  ten  dollars  ($10.00)  damages  in  Justice
Court,  Precinct  No.  1, Position  No.  2, Harris  County,  Texas.
Because  of the  amount  involved,  the  Justice  Court  was  the
only  Texas  Court  having  jurisdiction;  and  no  appeal  lies
from.  the  judgment  in  such  cases.
Article  5,  Section  19,
.Constitution  of  Texas;  Articles  2385  and  2454,  Revised

facts;

3.  That  Article  3107  of  the  Revised  Civil  Statutes  of
Texas  (construed  by this.  Court  in Nixon  v.  Condon,  supra)
provides  in part  that  ‘‘No  person  shall  ever  be denied  the
right  to  participate  in a  primary  in this  State  because  of
former  political  views  or  affiliations  or  because  of member-
ship  or  non-membership  in  organizations  other  than  the
political  party.’
~  That  the  immunities  thus  granted  to  the  classifications
of  ‘‘former  political  views  or  affiliations’’  and  **member-
ship  or  non-membership  in  organizations  other  than  the
political  party’?  have  been  held  valid  by  the  Supreme
Court  of Texas  in Love v.  Wilcox,  119  Texas  256, 28 S. W.
.(2d)  515;  arid  that  the  failure  of  the  Supreme  Court  of
Texas  to extend  the  same  immunity  to the  classification  of
‘‘race  and color”’ in the Bell v.  Hill case,  supra,  was  a denial
to  petitioner  and  all  other  qualified  Negro  voters  of  the
due  process  of law  and  equal  protection  of  the  laws  guar-
anteed  by the  Fourteenth  Amendment  and  an  abridgement
of  petitioner’s  right  to vote  in  violation  of  the  Fifteenth
Amendment  to  the  Constitution  of  the  United  States  (R.
9-10).

That  the  decision  of the  Supreme  Court  of Texas  in the

primary  elections.”

SUPREME  COURT  OF THE  UNITED  STATES
OCTOBER  TERM,  1934

R.  R.  GROVEY,

Petitioner,

vs.
ALBERT  TOWNSEND,

BRIEF  OF  PETITIONER  ON  APPLICATION  FOR

Decision  Below.

The  decision  below  is not  réported,  but  is printed  in  the

Record  at  pages  14  and  18-19.

Jurisdictional  Statement.

(a)  Statutory  Provision  Believed  to  Sustain  Jurisdiction.

First.  In  Texas,  there  are  three  courts  of  original  juris-
diction  ; District  Courts,  County  Courts,  and Justice  Courts;
District  Cour§s  and  County  Courts  also’ have  certain  appel-
late  jurisdiction.  The  jurisdiction  of  these  courts,  respec-
tively,  is  provided  for  in  Artice  5, Sections  8, 16,  and  19
of  the  Texas  Constitution;  and  Articles  1906-1911,  1949-

16
1960, and  2385-2387,  2454,  2460 of  the  Revised  Civil  Stat-
(These  provisions  are  printed  herewith  as
utes  of Texas.

_  Appendices  4, 5, 6, and  7, respectively.)

:

Second.  The  Justice  Court,  because*°of  the  amount  in-
.  volved,  was  the  only  court  having  jurisdiction  of  the  in-
stant  case  under  the  constitution  and  laws  of  Texas.
See
Appendices  4, 5, 6, and  7.

Third:  Because  of  the  amount  involved,  no  appeal  was
allowed  from  the  judgment  in the  instant  case  to any  other
court;  by the  constitution  and  laws  of  Texas, either  as  of
right  or  by. permission  of any  court  or. judge, after  applica-
tion  therefor.
See  Appendices  4, 5, 6, and  7; Gulf,  etc.  R.
Co.  v.  Rawlings,  80  Texas  579,  16  8.  W.  430;  Arrington  v.
People’s Supply  Co.,  (Tex.,Civ.  App.)  52 8. W.  (2d)  678;  26
Texas  Jurisprudence,  Sec.  82, p.  879.

The  law. in  Texas  with  regard  to  appeals  from  Justice
Court is stated  very  clearly in  Hudson  v.  Smith,  (Tex. Civ.
'  App.)  133  S.  W,  486  (writ  of  error  denied  by  Supreme
Court  of  Texas,  see  note  133  S.  W.  486),  as  follows  (p.
488):

eh

‘*Neither  the  Constitution  nor  the law  gives the  right
of appeal  from  judgments  of the  justices’  courts  when
the  amount  in controversy is not  more  than  $20,  exclu-
sive  of costs.  An  appeal  i is  not  a  matter  of right,  but
the  right  is  conferred  by the  Constitution  or  statute.
In  the  absence  of  such  grant,  the  right  does  not  exist.
A judgment  from  which  there  can  be no  appeal  is con-
clusive  between  the  parties,  unless  absolutely  void.
The  amount  in  controversy  in  the  case  wherein  the
judgment  is  sought  to  be  enjoined  was  less  than  $20,
ahd  therefore  within  the  exclusive  jurisdiction  of the
court  which  rendered  it, from  which  no  appeal  could
be taken.’’

The  appellate  jurisdiction  of the  Courts of Civil Appeals
and  the  Supreme  Court  of  Texas  is  found  in Article  5,

17

Sections 3 and  6 of the  Constitution  of Texas,  and  Articles
1/28  and  1819  of the Revised  Civil  Statutes  of Texas,  These
provisions  are  printed  herewith  as  Appendices  8 and  9 re-
spectively,
'  Fourth.  The  record  thus  disclosing  that  ‘‘a  final  judg-
ment  or  decree  has  been  rendered  or  passed  by the  highest
court’’  of Texas ‘‘in  which  a  decision  could  be had”  in the
instant  case,  this  Court.  has  jurisdiction  tosreview  the  de-
cision  below  upon  certiorari  under  Section  237  (b)  of the
Judicial  Code,  Section  344  (b)  of  the  United  States  Code;
because:

.

(1)  There  are  drawn  in question  provisions  of  the  Con-
Stitation  of the  State  of Texas,  which,  as  construed  by the
Supreme  Court  of  Texas  in Bell  v.  Hill,  74  8.  W.  (2d)<
113,  to  authorize  the  disenfranchisement  of  which  peti-
tioner  complains,  are  alleged  to violate  petitioner’s  rights.
under  the  Fourteenth  and  Fifteenth  Amendments  to  the
Constitution  of  the  United  States  (R.  10).  These  Texas
constitutional  provisions  thus  drawn  in  question  are  Sec-
tions  2 and  27  of  Article-1  and  they  read  as  follows:

‘*Sec,  2.  All  political  power  is  inherent  in  the  peo-
ple,  and  all  free  governments  are  founded  on  their
authority,  and  instituted  for  their  benefit,  The  faith  |
of  the  people  of Texas  stands  pledged  to  the  preserva-
tion of a Republican  form  of government,  and,  subject
to  this  limitation  only,  they  have  at  all  times  the  in-
alienable  right  to  alter,  reform  or  abolish  ‘their  gov-
ernment  in such manner  ag they may  think  expedient. ‘9
‘*Sec,  27.  The citizens  shall  have  the right,  in a peace-
able  manner,  to  assemble  together  for  their  common
good;  and  apply  to  those  invested  with  the  powers  of
government  for  redress  of  grievances or other  pur-
poses,  by petition,  address  or  remonstrance,’’

The  court  below  in the  case  at bar  sustained  the  validity

of  these  Texas  constitutional  provisions  so  construed.

3p

18

(2)  Petitioner  set  up  and  claimed  below  that  respondent
had  deprived  him  of his civil  right  to vote  in the  statutory
Democratic  primary  election  involved  in this  case;  that this
civil  right  to  vote  was  protected  against  infringement  by
the Fourteenth  and  Fifteenth  Amendments  to the  Constitu-
tion  of the United  States;  and  that  this civil right  had  been
denied  and abridged  by respondent  as  an officer  of the State
of Texas,  and  by agencies  of government  within  the  mean-
ing of  Nizon  v.  Condon,  286 U.  8. 73 (BR. 3, 8, 12).
-  These  Federal  claims  were  denied  by  the  court  below.

 (b)  Date  of Judgment  to  be  Reviewed.

The  judgment  which  petitioner  prays  this  Court  to review
was  entered  on  September  23,  1934  (R.  14);  and  petition-
er’s  motion  for  a  new  trial  was  overruled  on  September
25, 1934  (R. 18-19).  The  petition  for  the writ  of certiorari
was  filed  December  6, 1934.

Condon,  286 U. 8. 73.

(c)  Statement  of  the  Case.
This  cade  may  be  described  as  the  sequel  to  Nizon  v.
;
The  petitioner,  R.  R.  Grovey,  a  Negro  citizen  of  the
United  States  and  of  the  County  of  Harris  and  State  of
Texas,  brought  this  suit  in  Justice Court,  Precinct  No.  1,
Position  No.  2,  Harris  County,  Texas,  on  September  10,
1934,  against  the  respondent,  Albert  Townsend,  to recover
damages  for  wrongfully  depriving  petitioner  of  his  legal
right  to cast  an  absentee  vote  in the  statutory  Democratic
primary  election  involved  in this  case  (R. 1).

Petitioner  was  a  duly  qualified  elector  under  the  Con.
stitution  and  laws  of the  United  States  and  of the  State  of
Texas,  and  was  a  member  of and  believer  in  the  tenets  of
the  Democratic  Party  (R.  1).

The  respondent,  Albert  Townsend,  was  on  the  9th  day
of July,  1934,  the  duly elected,  qualified,  and  acting  county  .

19

elerk  of  Harris  County,  Texas,  and  as  such  was  on  said
date  conducting  absentee  voting  for  the  election  involved
in  this  case,  pursuant  to  the  mandates.of  Article  2956  of
the  Revised  Civil  Statutes  of Texas  (R. 3-6).

The  statutory  Democratic  primary  election  involved  in
this  case  and  mentioned  above  was  held  on  July  28,  1934,
pursuant  to  the  mandatory  requirements  of  Articles  3101

_*  and  3102  of the  Revised  Civil  Statutes  of Texas  (R. 3).

On  said July 9, 1934,  petitioner,  being  a qualified  elector,
as defined  by the laws  of Texas, except  that he was  a Negro,
presented  himself  in manner  and  form  as  required  by said
Article  2956 of  the  Revised  Civil  Statutes  of  Texas,  fully
complied  with  said  Article  2956,  and  requested  of respond-
ent  an  absentee  ballot;  but  respondent  refused  to  give  the
ballot  and  deprived  respondent  of ‘his  right  to  cast  an
absentee  vote in said  election  (R. 6-7).

The  respondent  gave  as  his  sole  and  ‘only  reason  for
denying  the  ballot  and  the  vote  the  fact  that  petitioner  was
a  Negro,  and  that  Negro  voters,  otherwise  qualified,  were
disenfranchised  in  said  eleetion  by virtue  of  instructions
to  that  effect  from  the  County  Democratic  Executive  Com-
_  mittee  of  Harris  County,  Texas,  and  by virtue  of.a  reso-
lution  adopted  May  24, 1932, by the  State  Democratic  Con-:
vention  of  Texas—meeting  pursuant  to  Article  3167  of
the Revised  Civil  Statutes  of Texas—which  resolution  reads
as  follows  (R.  7):

‘Be  it resolved,  that  all  white  citizens  of  the  State
of  Texas  who  are
qualified  to  vote  under  the  Corsti-
tution  and  laws  of  the  State  shall  be  eligible  to  mem-
bership  in  the  Democratic  Party  and  ‘as  such,  entitled  »
to  participate  in  its  deliberations’’  (the  primaries).
See  Bell  v.  Hill,  74 8.  W.  (2d)  113,  114,  and  Mason  v.
County  Democratic.  Executive  Committee,  74  S.  W.
(2d)  326, for  variations  in wording  of resolution.

20

Petitioner  in  his  original  petition  below  challenged  in
detail  the  authority  set  up  by respondent  for  his  acts  in
depriving  petitioner  of his  right  to cast  the  absentee  vote
as  being  a  violation  of  petitioner’s  rights  under  the  due
process  and  equal  protection  clauses  of  the  Fourteenth
Amendment  and  as  abridging  petitioner’s  right  to  vote  un-
der  the  Fifteenth  Amendment  to  the  Constitution  of  the
United  States  (R.  8-11).  The  contentions  of  petitioner  in '
this  regard  will  appear  more  fully  below  in this  brief.

Petitioner  alleged  that the Democratic  Party  is a national
.  political party,  which  does  not  exclude  Negroes  from  mem-
bership  in  it;  and  that  the  Democratic  Party  has  never
authorized  any  of  its  branches  or  agencies,  more  particu-
larly ‘the  State  Democratic  Convention  of  Texas,  to ex-
clude  Negroes  from membership in  the  Democratic  Party
‘(R.  11).

Petitioner  alleged  that  nominations,  equivalent  to  elec-
tion,  were  made in  the  statutory  Democratic  primary  elec-
tion  involved  in  this  case,  not  only  for  State  and  county
officers,  but  also  for United States Senator  and  Congress-
man  (R, 11).

Petitioner  alleged  that  the  respondent,  Albert  Town-
_  send,  was  a State  officer,  functioning  only under  State  laws,
whose  salary  was  paid  out  of  public  funds;  and  that  the
State  Democratic  Convention  of  Texas  and  the  County
Democratic  Executive  Committee  of Harris  County,  Texas,
are  agencies  of government-within  the meaning  of the  Four-
teenth  and  Fifteenth  Améndments  to  the  Constitution  of
the United  States  and Nizon  v.  Condon,  286 U. S. 73  (R. 12).

tion (R. 13).

Respondent  filed  a  general  @emurrer  to  petitioner’s  peti-  -
oe
‘The  court  below  sustained  the en  demurrer  and  dis-

missed  petitioner’s  action  (R.  14).

In  due  course,  petitioner  filed  a motion  for  a  new  trial,
in  which  he  again  raised  the  Federal  questions  set  up  in

.

(d)  Cases  Believed  to Sustain  Jurisdiction.
Certiorari  was  granted  in Nizon  v.  Condon,  286  U. 8. 73,
to  the  Circuit  Court  of  Appeals  for  the  Figgh  Circuit  to
review  identical  Federal  question’  involved  in  this  case.
The  identical  Federal  claim  sustained  by this  Court  upon
the  merits  in Nizon  v.  Condon,  supra,  was  denied  by  the
court  below  in the  case  at bar  (R,  14, 18).

The  authorities  are  unanimous  that  this  Court  has  juris-
diction  to review  a final judgment  of an  inferior  State  court,
where  no  appeal  of any  kind  lies  from  that  inferior  court’s
judgment.  —

The  general  proposition  is  stated  in  the  opinion  of  this:

Court  in Kentucky  v.  Powers,  201  U. 8. 1, 37, as  follows:

-

**But,  it is  said  that  the  action  of  the  trial  court  in
yefusing  to  quash  the  indictment  or  the  panel  of petit
jurors,  although  the  motion  to  quash  was  based  on  —
Federal  grounds,  cannot,  under  the  laws  of Kentucky,
be  reviewed  by the court  of appeals,  the  highest  court
If  such  .be  the  law  of  Ken-
of  that  commonwealth.
tucky,  as  declared  by the  statutes  and by the  court  of
appeals  of  that  commonwealth,  then,  after  the  case  is
disposed  of in that  court  by final  judgment,  in respect
of the  matters  of  which,  under  the  local  law,  it  may
take  cognizance,  a writ  of error  can  run  from  this  court
to  the  trial  court  as  the  highest  court  of  Kentucky  im
“which  a  decision  of the  Federal  question  could  be had;
and  this  court  in that  event,  upon  writ  of  error,  re-
viewing  the  final  judgment  of  the  trial  court,  can  ex-

In  the  ease  of  The  Moses  Taylor  v.  Hammons,  18 L.  Ed.
397,  suit  was,  as  in  the  instant  case,  brought  in  a  justice
court.  Under  the  California  law,  the  State  in  which  the
suit  was  brought,  an  appeal  lay  to the county  court,  whose
judgment,  on  account  of  the  amount  involved,  was  non-
appealable.
This  Court  reviewed  and  reversed  the judg-
ment  of  the  county  court,  saying  upon  the  jurisdictional
question  (p.  400):

‘*The.  decision  of  the  county  court  was  the  decision
of  the highest  court  in the State  which  had jurisdic-
tion  of  the  controversy.  -  From  that  court,  therefore,
the case is  brought  here by writ of error.’’

Other  cases  upholding  the same  principle  are:
Downham  v.  Alexandria,  19  L. Ed.  807.

broad  principle  as  follows:

**It  makes  no  difference  how minor  or  unimportant
the state court may be if its decision is final and there
is no  2  a court  having  jurisdiction.”

Summary  of Grounds  Upon Which Writ Is Sought.

Petitioner  prays  for  a  writ  of  certiorari  for  the  follow-
ing reasons  (Paragraph  5a  of Rule  38,  former  Rule  35, of  .
this  Court)  discussed  below  in this  brief:

(1).  The State  court  below-has  decided  a  Federal  ques-
tion  of  substance  not  thergtofore  decided  by this  Court—
a  Federal  question  expressly  left  open  by  this  Court,  in
Nizon  v.  Condon,  286  U.  S.  73,  ‘‘until  decision.  becomes
'
necessary.’’

(2)  The  State  court  below-has  decided  Federal  ques-
tions  of  substance  in  a  way  probably  not  in  accord  with
the  following  applicable  decisions  of this  Court:

‘

Nizon  v.  Herndon,  273  U.  8. 536.
Nizon  v.  Condon,  286  U. 8. 73.
Travis  v.  Yale  & Towne  Mfg.  Co.,  252  U.  8. 60.
Connolly  v.  Union  Sewer  Pipe  Co.,  184  U.  8.  540.
United  States  v.  Reese,  92  U.  S. Zt4.

(3)  Because  of their  importance  te  the  public  the  ques-
tions  involved  in this  case  should  be  settled  by this  Court.
Nearly  a  million  Negroes  are  now  disenfranchised  in
Texas,  and  about  ten  millions  in  the  South,  chiefly,  as  a
matter  of common  and  general  knowledge,  through  the  in-
strumentality  of  statutory  Deraocratic  primary  elections,
equivalent  to general  electiofs,  like  the  one  involved  in this
case;  and  such  disenfranchisement  is the  real  purpose  of
these  statutory  primary  creations.
Deciding  expressly  to
the  contrary,  the  Federal  question  ruled:  upon  by  this
Court  in  Nixon  v.  Condon,  supra,  the  Supreme  Court  of
Texas  has held  in Bell  v.  Hill, 74 8. W.-(2d)  113,  that  this
disenfranchtisement  of the  nearly  million  Negroes  of Texas  -
is  not  only  legal,  but  that  it  is  guaranteed  and  protected
both  by the  Constitution  of Texas  and the Constitution  of
the  United  States.  Two  Texas  Courts.of  Civil  Appeals

.

have  also  recently  held  that  this  disenfranchisement  must
stand,  in  spite  of  Nixon  v.  Condon,  supra.  The  court  be-
low  in  the  instant  case  has  followed  the  Texas  appellate
courts,  and  its  jurisdiction  was  exclusive  and  its  judgment.
final  and  non-appealable.

—

If  this  Court  declines  to  exercise  its  discretion to take
the  jurisdiction  in  this  case  which  by all’ the authorities  it
.  has,  not  only the  State of  Texas,  but  the  entire  South  will
.  obviously  consider  itself  entrenched  in  the  position  which
.  the  Texas  courts  have  already  expressed—that  the  disen-
franchisement  of  ten  million  Negroes  as  now  practiced  is
.  in full  accord  with  the decisions  of this  Court.  Color  should

not  be  lent  to  that  position  without  a  full  hearing  upon  .
the  merits  in this  Court.

power  today  without  restraint  by any  law  to determine
_its  own  membership,  we  are  not required  at  this  time

whereby  the  privilege  of  unf‘tered  choice  has  been  |

25

required  by  the  statute  if  nomination  is  to  be  made

(Article  3105) ; arid  that,  if heed is  to be given  to the
realities  of  political  life,  they are  now  agencies  of  the
State,  the  instruments  by  which  government  becomes
a  living  thing,  In that  view,  so  runs  the  argument,  a
party  is still  free  to define  for  itself  the  political  tenets
of  its  members,  but  to  those  who  profess  its  tenets,
there  may  be  no  denial  of  its  privileges.

‘*A  narrower  base  will  serve  for  our  jud&ment  in
the  cause  at  hand.  Whether  the  effect  of  Texas  legis-
lation  has  been  to  work  so  complete  a transformation
of  the  concept  of a  political  party  as a  voluntary  as-
sociation,  we  do not  now  decide. ». 4 Nothing i in  this  opin-
ion  is  to  be  taken  as.  carrying.  with  it  an  intimation
that  the  court  is  ready  or  unready  to  follow  the  peti-
tioner  so  far.  As  to that,  decision  must  be  postponed
until  decision  becomes .necessary.’’  (Italics  ours.)

>

Niaon  v.  Herndon  decided  two proposttions:

(1)  That  a  qualified  Negro citizen  has  the  same  ‘civil
right  to vote in  the statutory  Democratic  primary  elections
of Texas  as  he does  in a general election  of that  State.  .

Mr.  Justice  Holmes,  rendering  the  opinion  of  this  Court,
said  that  “‘the  same  reasons that  allow  a  recovery  for  deny-’
ing ‘the  plaintiff  a a  votevat a final  election  allow  it for  deny-
.  ing a  vote\pt  the primary  election  that  may  determine  the

final  result’’  (p.°540).

The  reason  that ~~ at recovery  for  denying  a  vote  at
a  final  or  general  election  is  not  that  the  defendant,  who
may  deny  the  vote,  is a  state  officer,  but  that  plaintiff  has  a
legal  civil  right  to  vote  in  the  election  and  the defendant
has  a legal  duty  not  to deny  that  right.  Substantial  allega-
tions  of  action  by the  state  may  be  necessary  to  give  the
Federal  courts  jurisdiction  to  redress  the  wrong,  but  the
.  presence  or  absence  of  the  state  in the  picture  does  not
determine  the  presence  or  absence  of  either  the  right  o
As  said  by  thj
plaintiff  or  the  duty  of  the  defendant.

‘‘An  individual  cannot  deprive  a  man  of  his  right
to  vote,  to  hold  property,  to  buy  and  to  sell,  to  sue  in
the- courts,  or  to  be  a_witness  of  a  juror;  he  may,  by
force  or  fraud,  interfere  with  the  enjoyment  of  the
right  in  a-particular  case;  he may commit  an  assault
against  the’ person  or  commit  murder,  or  use  ruffian
violence  at  the-polls  or  slander  the  good  name  of  a
fellow  citizen;  but,  unless  protected  in  these  wrongful
acts by some  shield«of  State  law  or  State  authority,  he
cannot  destroy  or  injure  the  right;  he will  only  render
himself  amenable  to  satisfaction  or  punishment; and
amenable  therefor  to  the  laws  of  the  State  where  the
wrongful  acts  are  committed.”’

ie a)  It  cannot  be  disputed  that  we. respondent,  who

His  office  of  county

His  powers  and  duties  are  fixed by statute.  Chapter  Two,
Title  41,  Articles  1935-1948,  Revised  Civil  “Statutes  of

of  public  funds:  Articles  3883,  3932,  and  1644  of  the  Re-

That  State  judicial  decisions  are  as  much  within  the
prohibition of  the  Fourteenth  and  Fifteenth  Amendments
as  legislative  enactments  has  been  well  settled  by  this
-Court.  Brinkerhoff-Paris  Trust  and  Savings  Company v
Hill,  281  U. 8S.  673,  680  (and cases  cited).

(c)  The  State  of Texas. has further  given  its Protection
to  the  deprivation  of petitioner’ s  civil  right  to  vote  by  the
decision of the  Supreme  Court  of Texas  in Bell  v.  Hill,  74
S.  W.  (2d)  113,  which  holds  that  the  deprivation  is  pro-
tected  aud guaranteed  by the First  Amendment  to the  Con-
stitution  of the  United  States  snd  Sections  2  and  27  of
Article 1 of the Constitution  of Texas  (pp.  119,  122).

Niaon v.  Condon,  supra,  went  off on  the  eine of fact
that,  assuming  a question  of  party  membership  to  be in-
volved,  no  authorized  voice  of  the  Democratic  Party  dad
yet  spoken  that  Negroes  could  not. be Democrats.  Specifi-
cally,  this  Court  held  as  a  matter  of  fact,  determined  by
_  “the  fundamental  postulates  of party organization,’’  that
the  Texas  State  Democratic  Executive  Committee  was  not
such  an  authorized  voice.

;

Petitioner  contends: that  there  is ‘nof  properly  involved
in this  case  any  question  of party  membership,  but‘that,  if.
there  is, the  instant  case  is on  all-fours  with  Niwon  v.  Con-
.  don, supra,  in  that  still  no  authorized  voice  of  the  Demo-
cratic Party has yet said  that  Negroes  could  not  be Demo-
crats;  but  that  on  the  contrary,  the  supreme  voice  of  the
Democratic  Party  has,  since  the  Texas  State  Democratic
Convention  passed the resolution  involved  in this case,  dedi-
cated  that  party  te  the  Jeffersonian  principle  of  ‘‘équal
rights  to all, special  privileges  to none.’*

(a)  The  Democratic  Party  is a  national  political  party;

it is not  a  State  political  party.

A  few  quotatiéns  from  Bryce’s  The  American  Common-

wealth  will  show  beyond  question  this historical  truth.

‘*What  constitutes  a  party?  In  Ameridarthere  } is  a
simple  test..  Any  section  of men  who nominate  candi-
dates  of  their  own  for  the  presidency  and  vice-presi-
dency  of  the  United  States  are-  deemed  a  national
party.  Adopting  this test, we shall find that  there  have
lately  been  two  or  three  national  parties  in agree to
the Republicans  and  Democrats.’’  (Bryce,
Ameri-
‘can  Commonwealth,  Vol.  2, p. 41.)

(Italics  ours.)

‘‘We  might  therefore  expect  that  the  State  parties
"would  be ‘as  distinct  from  the  national  parties. as are
the State goveramente from  the  Federal.

‘

‘

\.

i

=  i

»

ne

‘“‘The  contrary  has  happened.  The  national  parties
have  engulfed  the State  parties.  The  latteghave  dis-
appeared  absolutely  as-ifidependent  bodies,%and  sur-
vive  merely  as  branches  of the  nationa]  parties,  work-
ing each  in its  own’State  for  the  tenets  and  purposes
which  a  national  party  professes.and  seks  to attain.”’
(Vol.  1, p,  572.)
‘*  According]

national  parties  have  complete
possession  of  the  field.  In  every.State  from  Maine  to
Texas,  all  State  elections  for  the  governorship  and
other  offices  are  fonght  on  their  lines;  all  State  legis-  |
latures  are  divided  into  members  belonging  to  one  or
other  of them.  Every  trial  of strength  in-a  State  elec-.
tion  is  assumed  to presage  a  similar  result  in  a  na-
tional  election,  Every  State,  office  is deemed  as  fitting
a  reward  for  services  to the  national  party  as  for  serv-
ices  in  State  contests.  In fact,  the  whole  machinery  is
worked  exactly  as  if the  State  were  merely  a  subdivi-
sion  of the  Union  for  electoral  purposes.’’  (P.  574,
Italics  ours.)  |

_(b)  The  Democratic  Party  being  a  national  political
party,  its  supreme  voice  is  the’National  Democratic  Con-
vention.

In Nizon  v.  Condon,  supra, this  Court  said  thé following

(p. 84):
-™,
‘Whatever  inherent  power  a  State  political  party
 -has  to  determine  the  content  of  its  membership  resides
in the  State  convention.
(Bryce,  Modern  Democracies,
Vol.  2, p.  40.)’’

(Italics  ours.)

5

*

On  page  46 of  the  same  authority  (Bryce,  Modern  Dem-  .

ocracies,  Vol.  2), wyiting  to  the  same  point  with  reference
to  national  political  parties,  Viscount  Bryce  says:

‘**In  the  United  States  the  National  Convention  is the
suprethe  exponent  of  party  doctrine  and  policy,  uni-

'  versally  recognized  as  the  party  oracle.’’

-

36

-

“Whatever  inherent  power  the  Democratic  Party  has,
therefore,  to  determine  the  content  of  its  membership  re-
sides,  not  in forty-eight  separate  and  distinct  and  different
State  Democratic  conventions,  not  even  in the  State  Demo-
cratic, Convention  of  Texas,  as  would  be  true  if it were  a
State  political  party—but  that  inherent  power,  such  as
exists in  America  today,  resides  i in  the  National  Democratic
Convention.

Any  power  which  the  State Democratic  Convention  of
Texas  may  claim  to  determine  the  content  of  the  member-
ship  of  the Democratic  Party  must,  therefore,  necessarily
come  to it as  a  matter  of  delegation  from  the  National
Democratic  Convention.
’  Petitioner  alleged in  his  petition  below  that  no  power  to
exclude  Negroes  had  been  thus  delegated  (R.  11).  The

;

-  general demurrer  (R. 13), of course, concedes  this fact.

~

_.
Not  only  has  the  National  Democratic  Convention  not
-  delegated  any  such  power,  but  it  closed  its  platform
adopted  at  Chicago  on  June  27,  1932,  (subsequent  to  May
24,  1932,  the  date  of  the  resolution  of  the  Texas  State
Democratic  Convention  confining  membership  in the  Demo-
cratic  Party  to  white  people)  with  these  words:

**To  accomplish  these  purposes  and  to  recover  eco-
nomic  liberty  we  pledge  the  nominees  of  this  conven-
tion and  the  best  effort  of a  great  party  whose  founder
‘announced  the  doctrine  which  guides  us  now,  in  thee
hour  of our  country’s  need  ‘equal  rights  to  all, special
privileges  to  none.’’’  (World  Almanac,  1933,  p.  933.)
(Italics  ours.)

It  is a  matter  of  historical  and  general  knowledge,  too,
that  Negroes  were  seated i in the  National  Democratic  Con-
vention  of 1932.

It  is  also  significant  that  the  Texas  State  es
Convention  which  sought  by the  resolutioninvolved  in this
case  to confine  membership  in the Democratic  Party  to the

white  race,  was  meeting  pursuant  to  a  Texas  statute,  Ar-
ticle  3167  of  the  Revised  Civil  Statutes of  Texas, for  the
express  and  only purpose of electing delegates  to this same
National  Democratic  Convention  of  1932.
Court  of Texas  states,  in Bell  v.  Hill,  supra, at  p.  114,  that
such  was  the  nature  and  purpose  of  the  State  Democratic
Convention,  which  adopted  the  resolution  involved  in  this
case:

>

|

The  Supreme  ~

‘‘The  petition  states  in  substance, in. part,  that  the
respondents  are  denying  or will  deny,  the  relators  the
right  to  vote,  becanse  of  a resolution  passed  by- the
State  Convention  of  the  Democratic  Party  in  Texas.
on  May  24,  1932,  which’  reads:  ‘Be  it  resolved,  that
all  white  citizens  of the  State who  are  qualified  to vote -
under  the  Constitution  and  laws  of  Texas  shall  be
eligible  for  membership  in the  party, and as  such  elig-
ible  for participation in  the  primaries.’

‘‘This  resolution  was  passed  by the  Democratic  Con-
’  vention  which  met  at  Houston  for  the  purpose  of elect-  -
ing delegates  to  the  National  Convention  of the  Demo-
cratic  Party.”’

(c)  The  Texas  State  Democratic  Convention  had  no  in-
..  herent  power  by  virtue  of  its  being  to  say who  may  be

Democrats  and  who  may  not.

‘*The  National  Convention  is  the  supreme  exponent  of
party  doctrine  and  policy.””  Bryce,  Modern  Democracies,  .
supra.  As.explained  i in  Bryce’s  The  ‘American  Common-
wealth,  supra,  the  State  convention  in  Texas  is  merely  a
branch  or  subdivision  of  the  national  party,  and  as  such
is vested  only  with  the  management  of  the  party’s  affairs
in  a  single  State.
Except  when  duly  authorized  by  the
supreme  voice  of  the  Democratic  Party,  which  it was  not
in this  case,  it has  no  power  to  exercise  the  supreme  inher-
‘ent  power  of  the  Democratic  Party.  The  reasons  were
plainly  stated  by this  Court  in Nizon  v.  Condon,  supra  (p.
86),  as  follows:

‘

38

,

|  4

‘The  management  of the  affairs  of a  group  already
associated  togethér  as  members  of a party  is obviously
a very  different  function  from  that  of determining  who
the  members  of  the  group shall  be.
If  another  view
were  to  be  accepted,  a  committee  might  rule  out  of  the
'  party  a  faction  distasteful  to  itself,  and  exclide  the
very  men  who  had  helped to  bring  it into  existence.”’

Founded  by  Thomas  Jefferson  long  prior  even  to  the
independence  of  Texas  from  Mexico,  to  say nothing  of  its
statehood,  the  members  of  the  Democratic  party-were  al-
ready  associated  together  as  a  party  for  several  decades
_  prior  to  the  creation  of  the  State  Democratic  Convention
of  Texas.  It follows  then  that this  State  convention  could
not,  ipso  facto,  and  by  its  own  ipse dixit,  and  merely  by
receiving  the  privilege  of  itself  becoming  a  branch  of  the
Democratic  Party,  suddenly  as ascend  into pessession  by vir-
__tue  of. its being of ‘all of  the in inherent powers  of  the  party,
with full  authority  to rule  out even  those who  let  the’Texas
State  Democratic  Convention  into  the  Democratic  Par’ y.

_

‘(d)  The  Texas  State  Democratic  Convention  having
neither  delegated  nor  inherent.  power  to  make  race  and
color  a  test  for  membership  in  the  Democratic  Party,  it is
inescapable  that  whatever  power  it has  in  this  regard  has:
come  to  it  by  virtue  of  Sections  2 and  27  of  the  Bill  of
_  Rights  (Article  1) of the  Texas  Constitution,  as  construed
by the  Supreme  Court  of  Texas  in  Bell  v.  Hill,  74  8.  W.
|
(2d)  113.
In Nixon  v.  Condon,  supra,  this  Court  held that  the  State
Democratic  Executive  Committee  was  an agency  of the  State
in  depriving  Negroes  of  their  vote  because  that  committee
had  ‘‘excluded  petitioner  and  others  of  his  race,  not  by:
virtue  of  any  authority  delegated  by  the party,  but  by
virtue of an  authority  originating  or  supposed to  originate
in the  mandate of the  law.’’

‘

39

The  same  is true  of the  Texas  State  Democratic  Conven-
tion  in the  case  at  bar,  and that  convention  is likewise,  for
the  same  reason,  an  agency  of  the  State.  Speaking  of  the
convention’s  power,  the  Supreme  Court  of  Texas  said  at
page  122  of Bell v.  Hill,  supra:

‘“We  are  clearly  of  the*opinion  that  the  resolution:
passed  by the  Democratic  State  Convention  at  Hous-
ton  was  @  valid  resolution  under  the  power  clearly
guaranteed  to  that  body  by the  Bill  of — of thisy
State.’’  ‘(Italics  ours.)

In  Nixon  v.  Condon,  supra,  this  Court  said  of  the  s same
power  claimed  by the  State  Democratic  Executive  Commit-
tee,  the  following (p. 85):

“Tf  the  State  had  not  conferred  it,  there  would  be
hardly  color  of  right  to  give  a  basis  for  its  exereise.’’

Likewise,  when  the State  Democratic  Convention  of Texas  .

arrogates  unto  itself  this  same  power  without  authority
from  the  supreme  voice  of the Democratic  Party,  petitioner
‘respectfully  submits  that  ‘‘if  the  State  had  not’’  guaran-
teed  ‘‘it,  there  would  be-hardly  color  of  right  to  give  a

-  basis  for  its  exercise.’’

‘(e)  This  Court  is not  bound  by decisions  of the  Supreme  ~

Court  and  Court  of  Civil  Appeals  of  Texas  holding  that
the  Texas  State  Democratic  Corivention  is  not  an  agency
of  government.

In. Nixon  v,  Condon,  supra, this  Court said  the  following

(p. 88):

_**Whether  in  given  circumstances,  parties  or  their
committees  are  agencies  of government  within  the Four-
teenth  or  the  Fifteenth  Amendment is  a question  which
this  court  will  determine  for  itself.  Jt is  not  concluded
upon  such  an  inquiry  by decisions  rendered  elsewhere.’’
(Italics  ours.)  °

40

Not  only  is  this  ultimate  Federal  question  open  for  re-
view  by this  Court,  but  this  Court  may  also  decide  for
itself  every  other  question  which  inheres  in or  is necessary
_  toa  proper decision  of that  Federal  question.  Such  an  inci-
dental  question  is the  question  of whether  the  Texas  State
Demoeratic  Convention,  as  a  braneh  of  the  Democratic
Party,  has  the  inherent.  power  by,  virtue  of  its  being  to
make  race  and  color  a  test  of being  4 Democrat,  in the  very
teeth  of  a  subsequent  declaration  of  the  National  Demo-
cratic  Convention—which  Viscount  Bryce  says  is  ‘‘the  su-
preme  exponent’of  party  doctrine  and  policy’”—-dedicatis#®
the  Democratic  Party  to  a  policy  of  ‘‘equal  rights  to  all,
special  privileges  to  none.’’  ©

The  general  principle  of  this  Court’s  power  and  duty
to  decide  for  itself  such  questions  has  been  recently  re-
stated  by this  Court  through  Mr..Chief  Justice  Hughes  in
Sterling  v.  Constantin,  287  U.  S. 378,  397,  as  follows:

‘*If  this  extreme  position  could  be deemed  to be well
taken,  it is  manifest  that  the  fiat  of  a  state  Governor
‘and  not:  the  Constitution  of  the  United  States,  would
be the  supreme  law  of  the  land;  that  the  restrictions
of the  Federa]  Constitution  upon  the  exercise  of State
power  would  be- but  impotent  phrases,  the  futility  of
which  the  State  may  at any  time  disclose  by the  simple
process  of  transferring  powers  of  legislation  to  the
Governor  to  be exercised  by him,  beyond  control,  upon
his  assertion  of  necessity.  Under  our  system  of  gov-
‘  ernment,  such  a  conclusion  is  obviously  untenable.
There  is no  such  avenue  of escape  from  the  paramoun
authority  of  the  Federal  Constitution.  -When  there  is  ©
a  substantial  showing  that  the  exertion  of state  power
has  overridden  private  rights  secured  by the  Constitu-
tion, the  subject  is necessarily  one  for  judicial  inquiry
in  an  appropriate  proceeding  directed  against  the  in-
dividuals  charged  with  the  transgression.  To  such  a
case  the  Federal  judicial  power  extends  (Art.  III,  § 2)
and,  so  extending,  the  court  has  all the  authority  ap-,

41

propriate  to  its  exercise.  Accordingly,  it has  been  de-
cided  in  a  great  variety  of  circumstances  that  when
questions  of  law  and  fact  are  so  intermimgled  as  to
make  it  necessary  in order  to  pass  upon  the  federal
question,  the  court  may,  and should,  analyze  the  facts.
Even  when  the  case  comes  to  this  Court  from  a  State
Court  this  duty  must  be performed  as  a  necessary  inci-.
dent  to  a  decision  upon  the  claim  of denial  of Federal
right.’’

(Italics  ours.)

F

In  the  more  recent  case  of  Johnson  Oil Refiining  Com-

pany  v.  Oklahoma,  290  U.S.  158, 159, this  Court  said:

‘‘The  Supreme  Court  of  the  State  reached  the  con-
clusion  that  all  the cars  had  their  ‘taxable  situs’  at the
latter  place.  As  the  aSserted  Federal  right  turns  upon  ~
the  determination of the question of  situs,  it  is  our
_  province  to analyze  the  facts  in order  to apply  the  law,
and  thus  to  ascértain  whether  the  conclusion  of  the
state  court  has  adequate  support  in  the  evidence.’’

Besides,  the  question  involved  is  a  question  of  general
law  and  jurisprudence,  and  as  to  such  questions  this  court
has  always  reserved  the  right  to  exercise  its  own  independ-
ent  judgment.

|

—

Tyson,  10  L.  Ed.  865.

Swift  v.
Burgess  v.  Seligman,  107  U.  8. 20.
Sim  v.  Edenborn,  242  U.  8. 131.
See  also:  Barclay  v.  Hayes,  208  Fed.  319.

III.  Sections  2 and  27  of  the  Bill  of  Rights  (Art.  1)  of
'  the  Constitution  of  the  State  of Texas,  as  construed  by the
Supreme  Court  of Texas  in Bell  v.  Hill,  74 S. W.  (2d)  113,
and  as  sustained  by the  judgment  below  in the case  at  bar,
are  unconstitutional  for  the  reason  that  they  deny  to  the
classification  of  race  and  ¢olor  the  benefits  which  are  guar-
anteed  to  the  classifications  of  past  party  affiliations  and
membership  or  non-membership  in organizations  other than
political  parties  by Article  3107  of  Be Revised  Civil  Stat-

42

utes  of Texas as upheld  by  the  Supreme  Court  of Texas
in  the  case  of Love  v.  Wilcox,  28  S..W.  (2d)  515.  The  de-”
nial  by- the  judgment of the  apurt  below  of  this  claim  of
Federal  right  is not  in-accord  with  such  decisions  of  this
Court  as  Travis  v. Yale  &  TownggMfg.  Co.,  252  U.  8.  60,
and  Connolly  v.  Union  Sewer  Pipe  Co.,  184  U.  8. 540,  hold-
'  ing  that  where  one  class  is  favored  by exemptions  denied
to  another  class  under  the  state  law,  there  must  be  some
reasonable  basis  for  the  classification  thus  established  in
order  for  it to  meet  the  requirements  of  the  equal protec-
tion  clause  of  the  Fourteenth  Amendment.

|

That  race  and  color  cannot  be  made  the  basis  of  such  a

classification  has  been  well  settled by this  Court: p

‘‘States  may  do  a  good  deal -of  classifying  that  it j is

difficult  to  believe  rational,  but  there  are  limits, and
it is too  clear  for  extended  argument  that  color  cannot  ©
be made  the  basis  of a  statutory  classification  affecting
the  right  set -up-  in  this  case.’’  (P. 541 of  Nixon  v.
Herndon,  supra).

It  should  be kept in  mind  also  under  this  point  that  we
.are  not  dealing  with  a  convention  formed  at  the  ‘instance
or  suggestion  of private  individuals  upon  their  own  voli-
tion.  But, on  the contrary,  as  said  by United  States  District
Judge  Kennerly  in White  v.  The  County  Democratic  Execu-
tive  Committee,  supra,  ‘‘the  convention  itself  is a  creation
.of a  statute  which  fixes  the  time  of its meeting,  the purpose
of its meeting,  and  prescribes  in detail  the  manner  of choos-
‘ne  those  who participate  therein.
v-sed  Civil  Statutes  1925.’’  Moreover,  as  said  by the  Su-
preme  Court  of  Texas  in  Sterling  v.  Ferguson,.53  8S.  W.
(2d)  753,  760, the election  involved  is a eee primary
election.’’

Article  3167, Texas  Re-—

‘And  it is in such  a  statutory  election  and  to such  a  stat-
utory  creature  that  sections  2 and  27  of  the  Texas  Bill  of
Rights  guarantees  the  power  to  make  ‘race  and  color  a

»

:

;

43
qualification  and  test  of  voting,  while  Article  3107  of  the
Revised  Civil  Statutes  of  Texas  (held  valid  by the ‘Texas
Supreme  Court  in Love  v.  Wilcoz,  28 S.  W.  (2d) -515).ex-
empts  from  such  discrimination  the  classifications  of  past
party  affiliations  (the traditional  test  of party loyalty)  and
membership  or  non-membership  in organizations  other  than
the  political  party.
So,  whatever  may  be  the  rights  and  privileges  of  men
whose  own  minds
and volitions  bring  them  together,  at  a
time,  and in  a m
t,  and  for  purposes  which  they them-
selves  privately  decide  upon,  it is obvious  tliat  the  public
wellfare  demands  a  different  standard of duty  from  men
who  meet together  ‘‘by  virtue  of’  and  ‘‘under  the  terms
of’’  a  State  statute  (sée  Bell  v.  Au,  supra,  pp.  114,  121),
_  especially  when,  as such  a  ‘‘creation  of  a  statute’’  they
possess “‘ power clearly gu. ranteed”’  (see Bell v. H ill, supra,
p.  122)  by the  State  law  to  make  race  and  color a test  of
voting  in a  ‘‘statutory”’  election.
oy
IV.  The  decision  below  is  not  in  accord with  the  appli-
cable  decision  of  United  States  vy.  Reese,  92  U.  8.  214,:
where  construing  the  Fifteenth  Amendment,  this  Court
said  (p. 218):

ety

'

“If citizens  of one  race  having  certain  qualifications
are  permitted  by law  to  vote,  those  of  another  having
the same  qualifications  must  be.”’
In  every  instance  where  the  managers  of  Statutory
Democratic  primary  elections  have  sought  to deny partici-
pation  therein.  to  white  people  upon  grounds  inconsistent
with  the qualifications  and  tests  laid  down  in the  constitu-
tion  and  laws  of  Texas,  the  Texas  appellate  courts  have
prevented  the denial,  For examples  see  the following-cases:

Love  v.  Wilcox,  288,  W.  (2d)  515.  -
Briscoe  v. Boyle,  286  8. W.  275.
Clancy  v.  Clough,  30 8. W.  (2d)  569.

44

*  statutory  Democratic  primary  elections

On the  other  hand,  in every  case  whet te managers  of
Pave  denied  Ne-
groes  on  account  of  race  and  color  alone,  the  right  to  vote
therein,  the  Texas  appellate  courts  have  upheld  the  depri-
Vation.  For  examples,  besides the  case  “at‘bar,  see  the fol-
lowing cases:

~

Bell v.  Hill, 74 8. W.  (2d)  113.
White  v.. Lubbock,  30  S. W.-(2d)  722.
County  Democratic  Executive  Committee  v.  Booker,

53  S.  W.  (2d)  123.

i.

Sey

Conclusion.  -

’

Because  (a)  the case  comes  to this  Court  from  the highest
_  court  of Texas  ‘‘in  which  a decision  could  be had”,  and  (b)
because  the  judgment  of the  State  court  below  decided  a
Federal  question  of  substance  not  theretofore ,decided  by
this  Court—a  Federal  question  which  this  Gourt  expressly
left open in Nixon  v.  Condon  ‘‘unitil decision  becomes  neces-
'  -sary,’?  and  (c)  because  the  judgment  of  the  State  court
’  below  is not  in acc6rd  with  the  applicable  decisions  of  this
Court  set  forth  and  discussed  above in  this  brief,  and  (d)
hecause  the  questions | Tavolved _in  this  case  are  of  great
public  interest,  affecting,  as  they do,  the  franchise  rights
of approximately  one-sixth  of the  population  of Texas  and
approximately  one-third  of  the  population  of  the  South,
petitioner  prays  that  this  Court  may  exercise  its  discretion
favorably  by  granting  te writ  of  certiorari  applied  for
in this  case.

and escheats;  of  all  cases  of  divorce;  of  all  misdemeanors

equal  to or  exceed  in value  five  hundred  dollars;  of all suits, ,

rari,  and  all writs  necessary  to  enforce  their  jurisdiction.

remedy  or  jurisdiction  is not  provided  by law  or  this  Con-

late,  as  may  be  provided  by law.  (Sec.  8, Art.  5, adopted

places  as  may  be provided  by law.’’
