‘*The  district  court  shall  have  original  jurisdiction  in

‘*I.  Suits in  behalf  of the  State  to  recover  penalties,  for-

feitures  and  escheats.  .

**2.  Cases  of divorce  and dissolution  of marriage.

of character.

‘*4.  Suits  for  the  trial  of title  to land  and  for  the  enforee-

ment  of liens  thereon.

**5.  Suits  for trial  of right  to property  levied on by virtue
of any  writ  of execution,  sequestration  or  attachment,  when
the  property  levied  on  shall  be  equal  to  or  exceed  in  value
five  hundred  dollars.

‘*6,  Suits,  complaints  or  pleas,  without  regard to any  dis-
tinction  between law and  equity,  when  the  matter  on  contro-
versary  (controversy)  shall  be valued  at  or  amount  to  five
hundred  dollars  exclusive  of interest.

‘*7,  Contested  elections.

(Const.,  Art.  5.)”’

**Sec.  6..The  Legislature  shall  as  soon  as  a
. after  the  adoption  of this  amendment  divide  the State  into

’  respective  districts  at a  general  election,  for  a  (a)  term  of

APPENDIX  °.

Articles  1728  and  1819,  Revised  Civil  Statutes  of  Texas.’

**Art.  1728  (1521)  (940)  (1011)  Appellate  jurisdiction.

The  Supreme  Court  shall  have  appellate  jurisdiction  co-
extensive  with  the  limits  of the  State,  éxtending  to all ques-
tions  of law  arising  in the  following  cases  when  same  have

61

been brought  to.the  Courts  of  Civil  Appeals  from  ‘final
judgment  of  trial  courts:

‘1,  Those  in  which  the  judges  of the Courts  of  Civil
Appeals  may  disagree upon  any  question  of  law  material
to  the decision.

‘*2.  Those  im which  one  of  the  Courts  of  Civil  Appeals
holds  differently  from  a prior  decision  of  its  own,  or  of
another  Court  of  Civil  Appeals,  or  of the  Supreme  Court
upon  any  question  of law.

‘“*3.  Those  involving  the  construction  or  the  validity of

statutes  necessary to  a  determination  of the  case.

‘*4.  Those  involving  the  revenues  of  the  State.
‘<5.  Those in  which  the Railroad  Commission is  a  party.
‘6.  In  any  other  case  in which  it is made  to appear  that
an  error  of  substantive  law  has  been  committed  by  the
Court  of  Civil  Appeals  which  affects  the  judgment,  but.
excluding  those  cases  in which  the  jurisdiction  of the  Court
of Civil  Appeals is  made  final  by statute.  ©

‘*In  all  cases  where  the  judgment  of  the  Court  of  Civil
Appeals is  a  correct  one  and  where  the  principles  of  law
declared  in  the  opinion  of  the  court  are  correctly  deter-
mined,  the  Supreme  Court  shall  refuse  the  application;  in
all cases  where  the  judgment  of the  Court  of Civil  Appeals
is a  correct  one  but  the  Supreme  Court  is not satisfied  that
the  opinion  of  the  Court  of  Civil  Appeals  in  all  respects
has  correctly  declared  the  law, it shall  dismiss  the  case  for
want  of jurisdiction.

‘‘Provided  further  that  in  cases  of  conflict  named  in
Subdivision  2 above,  the  Supreme  Court  may,  in its  discre-
tion,  refuse  the  writ  of  error  where  the  court  is  in  agree-
ment  with  the  decision  of the  Court  of. Civil  Appeals  in the
case  in which  the application  is filed;  and  in cases  of  such
conflict  with  a  previous  opinion  of the  Supreme  Court,  the
Supreme  Court  may,  in its discretion,  without  the  necessity
of granting  the  writ  and  hearing  the  case,  reverse  and re-
mand  the  same  on  the application  for writ  of error.’’  (Acts
1927,  40th  Leg.,  p. 214, ch.  144.)

62

“Art.  1819.  (1589),  (996).  Jurisdiction  defined.

_

The  appellate jurisdiction  of the  Courts  of Civil  Appeals
shall  extend  to all  civil  cases  within  the  limits  of  their  re-
spective  districts  of which  the  District  Courts  and  County
Courts  have  or  assume  jurisdiction  when  the  amount  in
controversy  or  the  judgment  rendered  shall  exceed  One
Hundred  Dollars  exclusive  of  interest  and  costs.’’
(As
amended  Acts  1929,  41st  Leg.,  p.  68, ch.  33,  § 1.)

BRIEF  ON  THE  MERITS

Decision  Below

The  Decision  below  is not  reported,  but  is printed in  the  Record

at  pages  14 and  18-19.

Jurisdictional  Statement
(1)  Date  of Judgment  to  be  Reviewed
,  The  judgment  which  petitioner  prays  this  Court  to  reverse  was’
entered  on  September  24,  1934-(R.  14);  and  petitioner’s  motion
for  a  new  trial  was  overruled  on  September  25,  1934  (R.  18-19),
The petition  for  the writ of certiorari  was  filed  Dyenite  6, 1934,  .
and  was  granted  January 14, 1935.

(2)  Specific  Claims  Below
(a)  That  Sections  2 and  27  of  Article  1  (Bill  of  Rights)  of  the

Court  of Texas
the disenfranchisement
of which  petitioner  complains,  violate  pe-
titioner’s  rights  under  the Fourteenth  and Fifteenth  Amendments

7

.

The court below in  the case at bar sustained  the validity of these
provisions  so construed.  ~

Texas constitutional

(6)  Petitioner  set up  and claimed  below that respondent  had de-
prived  him of his civil  right to vote in the statutory  Democratic
primary  election  involved  in this case; that this civil right to vote
was protected  against  infringement  by the Fourteenth  and Fifteenth
Amendments  to the Constitution  of the United  States;  and that thie
civil  right  had been denied  and abridged  by respondent  as an of-
ficer of the State  of Texas,  and by agencies  of government  within
the meaning  of Nixon  v.  Condon,  286 U:  S. 73 (R. 3, 8, 12).

(c)  Petitioner  claimed  that  he had  a  right,  created  by the Con-
stitution  of the United  States,  to vote for United  States  Senator  and
Representative  in the election  involved  in this case, and that re-
spondent  had deprived  petitioner  of that right. (R. 7, 11).
These  Federal  claims  were denied  by the court  below.
_
(3)  Statutory  Provision  Believed  to Sustain  Jurisdiction
‘First.  In Texas,  there are three courts  of original  jurisdiction:
District  Courts,  County  Courts,  and Justice  Courts;  District  Courts
and County  Courts also have certain  appellate  jurisdiction.  The
jurisdiction of these courts, respectively, is provided  for in Article

5, Sections  8, 16, and  19  of the Texas  Constitution;  and Articles
.  1906-1911,  1949-1960,  and  2385-2387,  2454,  2460  of  the  Revised
Civil Statutes of Texgs, 1925. (These provisions are printed herewith
,
as Appendices  4, 5, 6, and 7, respectively.)

Second.  The Justice  Court,  because  of the amount  involved,  was  -
the only court having  jurisdiction  of the instant  case under the
constitution  and laws of Texas.  See Appendices  4, 5, 6, and 7.

Third. Because  of the amount involved, no appeal  was allowed
from  the judgment  in the instant  case to any other court,  by  the
constitution  and laws of Texas,  either  as of right  or by permission
of any court or judge,  after  application  therefor.  See Appendices
:  4, 5, 6, and 7;  Gulf, ete.  R.  Co.  v.  Fwlings,  80 Texas 579, 16 S. W.
430;  Arrington  v.  People’s  Supply  Co.,  (Tex.  Civ.  App.)  52  S. W.
Sec. 82, p. 879.
(2d)  678;  26 Texas Jurisprudence,

The law  in Texas  with regard  to appeals  fromi Justice  Courts.  is
stated  very  clearly  in Hudson  v.  Smith, (Tex.  Civ.  App.)  133  S. W.
486 (writ of error denied  by  Supreme  Court of Texas, see note 133
S. W.  486),

as follows  (p. 488) :

“Neither the Constitution  nor the law gives the right of ap-
peal from judgments  of the justices’  courts when the amount
in controversy  is not more than $20, exclusive  of costs.  An
appeal  is not a  matter  of right,  but the right  is conferred  by
the Constitution
In the absence  of such grent, the
or statute.
right
does not exist.  A  judgment  from  which there can be no
appeal  is conclusive  between  the parties,  unless  absolutely
void.  The amount  in controversy  in the case  wherein  the judg-
ment  is sought  to be enjoined  was less than $20, and therefore
within  the exclusive  jurisdiction  of the court  which  rendered  it,
from  which  no appeal  could  be taken.”

The appellate jurisdiction  of the Courts of Civil  Appeals and the
Supreme  Court of Texas is found in Article 5, Sections 3 and 6, of
the Constitution  of Texas, and Articles  1728 and 1819 of the Revised
are printed  here-
of Texas,  1925.  These  provisions
Civil  Statutes
8 and 9, respectively.
with as Appendices

Fourth..  The record thus disclosing  that “a final judgment  or de-
cree has been rendered  or passed by the highest  court”  of Texas
“in which  a  decision  could be had” in the instant  case, this Court’
to review  the decision  below upon certiorari under
has jurisdiction

(4)  Cases  Believed  to  Sustain  Jurisdiction

Jurisdiction  was  held  to  exist  in  Nixon  v.  Herndon,  273  U.  S.
-  $36,  and  in  Nixon  v.  Condon,  286  U.  S.  73,  to  review  identical
The  identical  Federal
Federal  questions  involved  in  this  case.
claims  sustained  by this  Court  upon  the merits in the  Nixon  Cases
were denied  by the court below in the case at bar (R.  14,  18). ’

The  authorities  are  unanimous  that  this  Court  has  jurisdiction
to  review  a  final  judgment  of an  inferior  state  court,  where  no  ap-
'
peal  of any  kind  lies from  that  inferior  court’s  judgment.
The  general  proposition  is stated  in  the  opinion  of  this  Court  in

Kentucky  v.  Powers,  201  U.  S.  1, 37,  as  follows:

“But,  it is said that the action  of the trial  court  in refusing  to
quash  the  indictment  or  the  panel  of  petit  jurors,  although  the
motion  to  quash  was  based  on  Federal  grounds,  cannot,  under
the  laws  of Kentucky,  be  reviewed  by the  court  of appeals,  the
highest  court  of  that  commonwealth.
If  such  be  the  law  of
Kentucky,  as  declared  by the  statutes  and  by the  court  of  ap-
peals  of  that  commonwealth,  then,  after the case  is  disposed
of in that  court  by final  judgment,  in  respect  of the  matters  of
which,
a writ of
under  the local  law,  it  may také cognizance,
error can run from  this court to the  trial  court as the highest
‘court  of  Kentucky  in  which  a  decision  of the  Federal  question
could  be had;  and  this  court  in  that  event,  upon  writ  of error,
reviewing  the final judgment  of the trial  court,  can exercise  —
such  jurisdiction  in  the  case  as  may  be  necessary  to  vindicate
any  right,  privilege,  or  immunity  specially  set  up  or  claimed
under the Constitution  and laws of the United  States,  and in
respect  of which the decision  of the trial court  is made final
by the  local  law;  that  is, it may  re-examine  the  final  judgment
of the trial court so far as it involved  and denied  the Federal
right,  privilege,  or  immunity  asserted.  This  must  be  so,  else  it
will be in the power of a state to so regulate  the jurisdiction  of
its  courts  as  to  prevent  this  court  from  protecting  rights  se-
cured  by the  Constitution,  and  improperly  denied  in  a  subor-
dinate  state  court,  although  specially  set  up  and  claimed.”

In the  case  of The  Moses  Taylor  v.  Hammons,  18  L.  Ed.  397,

suit  was,  as  in  the  instant  case,  brought  in  a  justice  court.
Under
the  California  law,  the  state  in  which  the  suit  was  brought,  an  ‘ap-
peal  lay to  the  county  court,  whose  judgment,  on  account  of  the
amount  involved,  was  non-appealable.
This  Court  reviewed  and
reversed  the  judgment  of  the county  court,  saying  upon  the juria-
dictional question  (p. 400) :

“The  decision  of  the  county  court  was  the  decision  of  the’

highest  court  in the  State  which  had  jurisdiction  of the  contro-
versy.  F rom thet court,  therefore,  the  case  is brought here  by
writ of error.”

‘Other cases  upliolding  the  same  aN  are:

Downham  v.  Alexandria,  19 L.  Ed.  807.

Missouri,  etc.  v.  Elliott,  184  U.  S.  530.

Western  Union  v.  Hughes,  203  U.  S.  505.

Section  3214  of  Hughes’  Federal  Practice  states  the  broad  prin-

ciple as  follows:

“It  makes  no  difference  how  minor  or  unimportant  the  state
court  may be if its decision is  final  and  there is  no higher  court
having  jurisdiction.”

Statementof  the  Case

}

%

First.

This  is  the  fourth  time  that  a  Negro  citizen  has  found  it
necessary  to  apply-to  this  Honorable  Court  for  the  protection  of his
civil  right  to  vote  in  the  statutory  Democratic  pHimary  elections  of
Texas.
'
(a)  In Love  v.  Griffith  (decided  October  20,  1924),  266  U. S. 32,
the  question,  was  moot  before  it reached  this  Court,  but  this  Court
said that  “If  the  case  stood  here  as  it stood  before  the  court  of  first
instance  it would  present  a  grave  question  of constitutional  law  and
we  should  be astute  to  avoid  hindrances  in the  way  of taking  it up.”
(b)  In  Nixon  v.  Herndon  (decided  March  7, 1927),  273  U.  S.
-7936,  the  question  was  not  moot  and  the  Negro’s  constitutionally
protected  civil  right  to  vote  in  the statutory  Democratic  primary
elections  of ‘Texas  was  established  beyond  doubt  and  a  Texas  statute
which  sought  to  destroy  the  right  was  declared  unconstitutional,  this
Court saying  that “it is too  clear  for  extended  argument  that  color
cannot  be  made  the  basis  of  a  statutory  classification  affecting  the
right set  up  in this  case.”  (Italics  ours.)

inquiry  by decisions  rendered  elsewhere.”

(d)  Following  the second  Nixon  Case, Negroes  were again  de
prived  of their civil right to vote in the statutory  Democratic  pri-
mary elections  of Texas, and the instant  case presents the fourth
challenge  to the constitutionality
of that deprivation,  under  the
Fourteenth  and Fifteenth  Amendments  to the Constitution  of the
‘United  States.

Resolusion  Involved

Second:  In Nixon  v.  Condon,  supra,  as proof  that  the  State
Democratic  Executive  Committee  of Texas  had no power by virtue
“of its being to say what citizens could become  Democrats  and what
citizens  could not,  this Court made  the following  statement  at page
84:  “Whatever  inherent  power  a  State  political  party has to deter-
mine the content  of its membership  resides  in the State convention.
Bryce,  Modern  Democracies,  vol. 2, p. 40.”  (Italics  ours)

Assuming  that  the Democratic  Party is a  “state political  party”
—which  petitioner  alleges  that it is not—and  placing  a  favorable
significance  upon  the word “Whatever”  in  the  above  quotation
from this Court, the State Democratic  Convention  of Texas prompt-
ly passed,  on May 24, 1932, the following  resolution  (R. 7):

_  “Be it resolved,  that all white citizens  of the State  of Texas
who are qualified  to vote under the Constitution  and Laws  of
the State shall  be eligible  to membership  in the Democratic
party  and as such eligible  for participation  in its delibera-
See Bell v. Hill,  74  S.  W..(2d)  113,
tions”  (the  primaries).

7

and  Mason  v.  Commitiee,
ference  in reading  of resolution  may be found.

74 S. W. (2d)  326,  where  this dif-

State Constitutional  Provisions  and Court Decisions  Challenged
in Instant  Case
Third.  Since its adoption  and the deprivation  under its authority
of the legal right of Negroes  to vote in the statutory  Democratic
primary  elections  of Texas,  the above resolution  has been before
two Texas Courts of Civil Appeals  and the Supreme  Court of Texas.
County  Democratic  Executive Committee  v.  Booker,  53  S.  W.  (2d).
123; Bell v.  Hill, 74, S. W.  (2d)  113;  and Mason  v.  County  Demo-
cratic Executive  Committee,  74  S. W.  (2d)  326.
These cases are
tacitly relied upon by the court below as authority for the judgment
in the instant  case.

(a)  In the Booker Case (Cobbs,  J. dissenting)  the Court of Civil
Appeals at San Antonio,  through  Special  Justice Searcy,  thought
that its. decision  upholding  the resolution  was “in, line with the
opinion  of the Supreme  Court  of the United  States  in Nixon v.
Condon.”  Expressing  the same thought  the late Chief Justice  Fly,
in a  concurring  opinion,  said (p. 129) that Nixon v.  Condon,  “af-
ter destroying
the ship’ of party  power’ and rule, threw out a
spar upon which the party might keep its head above  the tumultuous
political  sea of 1932, that spar being the convention  of the party,
not acting  on any State  mandate,  but on its unfettered  volition.”
But this spar was discovered  only after the Chief Justice  had thus
written  (p. 129):  “However  much  the logic and strength  of the
minority  opinion in the case of Nixon v.  Condon, herein cited,  may
excel those of the majority  opinion,  however  much we may differ
with the majority  opinion,  representing  only five of the nine judges
of the Federal  Supreme  Court, still’ it ‘is the law of the land, and
this Court will endeavor  to follow not only  its spirit but its letter.”
(6)  The Mason  Case merely  followed  the Booker  and Bell Cases.
(c)  In the Bell Case, decided  July  20, 1934, the Supreme  Court
of Texas gave its approval  to the Booker  Case, but expressly  dis-
agreed with this Court’s decision in Nixon v.  Condon,  supra, saying
through  Chief Justice  Cureton  (p. 121) :

“Justice  Cardozo  held  that a proper  construction
of this
statute  was that the power  to  declare  the membership  of the
party had been conferred  upon the State Committee by law,

and  that the  effect  of  this  was  to  make  the  committee  a  state
agency,  which  could  not  discriminate  between  the  white  and  '
Negro  races  in fixing  the  membership  of the  Democratic  Party.
_  With this construction  of this Texas  statute  we cannot  agree.
The  State  Executive  Committee  of  the  Democratic  Party  in
Texas  is just  what  its  name  implies—a  committee  clothed  with
executive  power  to carry  out  the will  of the party.”  (Italics  ours.)
The  Supreme  Court  of Texas  further  held  that,  if  this  Court’s
construction  of  Article  3107  of the  Revised  Civil  Statutes  of  Texas
should  prevail  upon  the  proposition  of  whether  the  State  Demo-
cratic  Executive  Committee  was  an  agency  of  government  within
the  14th  or  15th  Amendment  (which  this  Court  said  in  Nixon  v.:
Condon  it  “will  determine  for  itself”),  then  said  statute  would  be
“plainly  void, because in  violation  of sections  27 and  2 of the  Bill  of
Rights”  (Article  l)  of the  Texas Constitution,  which  read  as  fol-
lows  (R.  10):

“Sec. 2.  All  political  power  is  inherent  in  the  people,  and
all  free  governments  are  founded  on  their  authority,  and  in-
stituted  for  their  benefit.  The  faith  of  the  people  of  Texas
stands  pledged  to  the  preservation  of  a’  republican  form  of
government,  and,  subject  to  this  limitation  only,  they  have  at
all  times  the  inalienable  right  to  alter,  reform  or  abolish  their
government  in‘such  manner  as  they  may  think  expedient.”

“Sec.  27.  The  citizens  shall  have  the  right,  in  a  peaceable
manner,  to
e  together  for  their  common  good;  and
apply  to  those  inyested  with  the  powers  of  government  for
redress  of  post
or  other  purposes,  by  petition,  addrese
or  remonstrance.”

The  Supreme  Court  of  Texas  further held  (p. 119)  that  the power
thus to  discriminate  against  Negroes  was  guaranteed  and  protected
by “the  first  section  of  the  Bill  of  Rights,  the  First  Amendment  to
the  Co~stitution  of  the  United  States.”

Petitioner  challenges  said  Sections  2  and  27  of  Article  1  (Bill
of  Richts) of  the  Constitution  of  Texas,  thus  construed,  and  the  de-
cisions.  construing  them,  as  violating  petitioner’s  rights  under  the.
Fourteenth  and  Fifteenth  Amendments  to  the Constitution  of  the
United States  (R.  10).

.
Petitioner’s  Injury
Fourth.  On  July  28,  1934,  a  statutory  Democratic  primary  elec-

.

tion was held in. the State of Texas, of the same character as the
ones involved  in Nixon v.  Herndon  and Nixon v.  Condon, supra, at
which nominations,  equivalent  to election,  were  made  of  United
_  States Senator,  United States Congressman,  and state, district, county

and  precinct  officers.

In  obedience to the mandate of Avticle 2956 ef the Revised Civil
Statutes  of  Texas,  absentee  voting  was being conducted  on July 9,
1934,  by the respondent,  Albert Townsend, who was the duly elect-
ed, qualified,  and acting county clerk of Harris County, Texas.  Said
article 2956 is quoted in full in petitioner's original petition (R. 4),
the pith of  which reads:

“Art.  2956.  Absentee  Voting. Any qualified  elector,  as defined
by the laws of this State, who expects  to be absent from the
County  of his or her residence  on the day of election  may vote
to wit:  At some time, not
subject  to  the following  conditions,
more than twenty (20)  days nor less than three (3)  days prior
to  the date of  such election, such elector  shall  make his or her
_.personal  appearance  before the Clerk of the County  of his or
_  her residence  and shall deliver  to such Clerk his or her poll
tax receipt  or  exemption  certificate  entitling  him or her to
vote at  such election,  and said Clerk shall deliver  to such eleo
tor  one ballot  which has been prepared  in accordance  with the
law  for  use in  such  election ....  The provisions  of this article
shall  apply  to  all  elections,  including  General, Special, -and
Primary  elections.”

.

was on said July 9,  1934,

a “qualified  elector, as
’  The  petitioner
defined  by the laws of” Texas;  was a  member  of and believer  in.
the tenets of  the Democratic  Party;  expected  to be absent  from his
county  on  said  election  day;  made  his  personal  appearance  before
the  respondent  and  delivered  to  respondent  petitioner's  poll  tax
receipt  entitling petitioner  to vote in  the election  involved  in. thie
case;  but  respondent  refused  to  deliver to petitioner  a  ballot or te.
permit petitioner  to vote in said election,  respondent  giving  as his
sole  reason and  justification  the fact that petitioner  was a  Negra,
and that under  the above quoted resolution  of the State Democratie
Convention  of  Texas and under  instructions  from  the County Dem
ocratic  Executive  Committee  of  Harris  County,  Texas,  petitiones
was ineligible  to vote in said election, solely and only because  pe
titioner  was a  member  of the Negro race.

10

!

‘ay Instant Suit Brought

“Fifth, Feeling himself aggrieved and damaged by  his said die

enfzanchisement  in the sum of Ten Dollars  (ten times the damages
awarded  by the jury  in Nixon  v.  Herndon,  supra;  ten  times  the
amount  agreed  as the measure  of damages  by the parties in  Nixon
v.  Condon,  supra;  and twice the amount  of damages  awarded  upon
a  trial to the United States District  Judge without  «  jury in Nixon
v.  McCann,
No. 1660 Law, U.  S. District  Court, Western  District
of Texas,  decided  February 7, 1934,  but not officially  reported;
twice  the amount  of damages  awarded  by the jury in Bliley  v.  West,
42 F. (2d)  101;—+ee  certified  copies  of judgments  in Nixon  Cases
printed  herewith  as Appendices  1, 2, and 3), petitioner,  on Septem-
ber 10, 1934,  brought  suit  for ten dollars  ($10.00)  damages  in
Justice Court, Precinct  No. 1,  Position  No. 2, Harris  County,  Texas.

The  election  involved  in this  case  is  an  election  which
was  held  under  statutory  compulsion,  which  was  wholly
pw  mem  and  in  which  those  charged  with  its  manage-
and  judicial
‘ment—have-been-  deprived  by  Texas  statutes
decisions  of all power  to fix  qualifications and tests  for
participation  therin,  inconsistent  with  the  qualifications
and  tests  fixed  by Texas  laws;  except  in  the  one  instance
where  such, ~My have  been  “guaranteed”  the  power
to deny  Negroes the right  to participate in such  election.

.  No principle is more firmly-settled  by the decisions o  this Court
guaran-
than the doctrine  that, in determining
ties have been violsted,  this court will  look “to substance,  not to
form,”  Chicago  B. & Q. R. Co.  v.  Chicago, 166  U.  S. 226,  234-235;

in

_  and  that states  cannot  escape  the- paramount  authority  of the  Con-
stitution  of the  United States  by tacking  upon  that  which is consti-
tutionally  prohibited  the  label  of that  which  under  certain  circum-
-  stances this Court Gnay find  to  be constitutionally  permissible.

Thus,  when  a  Governor  of  Texas  sought  to  escape  from  con-
stitutional  restraint  by. magking his conduct  behind the label “martial
law,”  this  Court  held  in  Sterling  v.  Constantin,  287  U. S'  378,
397,  that  “There  is no  such  avenue  of escape  from  the paramount
authority  of  the  Federal  Constitutiott.”
Likewise,  when in Life  & Casualty Insurance-Co.  of Tennesive  v.
McCray,  291  U. S.  566,  573,  this  Court  was urged  togdetermine  the
validity  of a  statutory  exaction  by the  label  “penalty,”  it was  held.
that “the  measure,  not  the  name  controls,”.

So,  also, if the election  involved  in the case at  bar  is measured
by its nature  instead  of its name,  it will  be found  to  be no different
in statutory  substance  from  the  election  labeled  ‘igeneral  election,”
which  followed  on  November  6, 1934;  A comparison  between  the
two  elections  will  demonstrate  this to  be  true.

 (b)  In  Koy v.  Schneider,  218 S.  W..47% the Supreme  Court of.

Texas had before  it a statute  which the court described  as follows

women possessing  the qualifications,  save of sex, of electors under
the Constitution  and laws of the state, at any and all primary  elec-
tions or nominating  conventions  to  be held under the laws of the
state, and requires  each woman  offering  to vote in any primary
election  ox convention,  after January  1, 1919, to comply with all

right to vote upon women. The court held that (p. 483) :

“There  being  no  constitutional  prohibition,  it follows  that
the Pegislature  was free to extend  the privilege  of participation
in  party  primaries and conventions to women  possessing  the
save sex, of.electors  under  our  Constitution  ard

Art.  2955.  “Every  person  subject  to  iioné  of  the  foregoing

Art.  2956—“Any  qualffied  elector,  as defined  by the  laws  of
thig  state, who expects  to  be  absent  from  the  county  of his  or  her
residence  on  the  day of election  may  vote  subject  to  the  following
conditions,  to  wit;”  (Italics  ours.)  This  is the  Absentee  Voting  Sec-
tion, is the section involved in this case, and is quoted in full in .
the  record  at  page  4).:
;

These  statutory  qualifications  have  been  respected  in  Texas  in
every case, except where Negroes  offered  to  vote. For  instance, thé
Bitorney-  General  of  Texas  handed  down  an  opinion  during  the
summer  of 1934, holding  that Mexicans  had a  right to vote in the
election  involved  in this case.  He also  handed  down  an  opinion  hold-
ing that Negroes  had no such right. See Bell v.  Hill, 74S.  W.  (2d)
113,  120.  The  reason  why the Attorney  General  of Texas  should  be
defining  the right to vote in the election involved in this: case,—vif
it is the election of a private  association,—is  the  simple  fact that  it
is not the electio#of a  private association,  but is “solely the creature
of the statute.”  Hamilton  v.  Davis,  supra.

aa

Besides,  the  election  involved  in  this case,  though  bearing
name of the sham battle, was in fact the real battle;  and the election

labeled  the real battle was in fact the sham battle.  It  was so alleged
below  (R. 11). It is a  matter of common  and
by the petitioner
general  knowledge  of whith this Court may take  judicial  notice,
Muller v.  Oregon,  208 U. S. 412, 421. It is commonly
and generally

this case for Governor,  for instance,  was 994,003.  (Houston  Chroni-

in the election  labeled  “general  election,”  for  governor  was  437,077
(House  Journal,  44th  Tex.  Leg.,  Reg.  Sess.,  Jan.  11,  1935,  p.  64).
It is also commonly  and generally  known that, since the creation  of
_  the statutory  primary  elections  of Texas, the statutory  Democratic
primary  elections  have always been the real battles and the general
elections  the sham battles. It is this commonly  and generally  known
fact  which  explains  why  Old  Article  3107  of  the  Revised  Cr:il
.  Statutes  of Texas, stricken down by this Court in Nixon v. Herndon,
supra, only sought to disenfranchise  Negroes in the statutory  Démo-

Petitioner  does not question  the right of the Texas appellate courts
to construe  the statutory  primary  election  laws of the state. But
when  those  laws, as  said by this Court in  Yick  Wo  v.  Hopkins,
118  U. S. 356, 373, are construed  “with an evil eye and.an  unequal
hand, so as practically  to make upjust and illegal -discriminations
between  persons  in similar circumstances,  material  to their rights,”
then petitioner  feels justified  in asking this Court to measure  the
election  involved  in this case, not by what the state may call it, but
by what it is in fact—a  creature  of the statute—and  to enforce against
the election  involved in this case not merely the constitutional  guar-

26

4

’

That  the state  of Texas  has participated  in and  given  its protection
to  the  deprivation  of  petitioner’s  civil  right  to  vote  in  the  statutory
Democratic  primary  election  involved  in  this  case  is likewise  clear.
(a)  It  cannot  be  disputed  that  the  respondent,  who  denied  the
vote,  was  a  state  officer.  His office  of County  Clerk is  created  by the
Texas  constitution  (Art.  5,  sec. 20).  His  powers  and  duties  are
fixed  by statute.  Chapter  Two,  Title 41,  Articles  1935-1948,  Revised
Civil  Statutes  of  Texas.  His  compensation  is  fixed  by  statute  and
comes  out  of public  funds.  Articles  3883,  3932,  and  1644  of  the
Revised  Civil  Statutes of Texas.  His powers  and  duties as the sole
conductor  of  absentee  voting  in  Harris  County,  Texas,  are  wholly
fixed by  statute.  Article 2956 of the Revised  Civil Statutes  of
Texas.  Indeed,  absentee  voting  was  unknown in  Texas  prior  to  its
creation  by statute.  See Sterling  v.  toate (oon, Sup.),  3 S. be
(2d)  753,  760.

(b}  The  judgment  of  the court  below  hint to  petitioner,  on
account  of  race  and  color  alone,  redress  for  the deprivation  of his
civil  right  to  vote  in the statutory- Democratic primary election
involved in this case is just as  much  a protection  of the  deprivation
by the State  of Texas  as  was  the  statute  stricken  down  by this  Court
in Nixon  v.  Herndon,  supra.
That  state  judicial  decisions  are  as  much  within  oe prohibition of
the  Fourteenth  and  Fifteenth  Amendments  as  legislative  enactments
has  been  well  settled  by  this  Court.  Brickerhoff-Faris  Trust  and
Savings  Company  v.  Hill,  281  U.  S.  473,  680  (and  cases  cited).
(c)  The State of Texas has further  given its protection  to  the
déprivation  of petitioner's  civil right to vote by the decision  of
the Supreme  Court of Texas in Bell v.  Hill,  74S.  W.  (2d)  113,
which holds that the deprivation  is protected  and guaranteed  by the
First  Amendment  to the Constitution  of the United States and Sec-
tions 2 and 27 of Article  1 of the Constitution  of Texas  (pp.  119,
122).

ane

?

_  The  17th  Amendment  to  the  Constitution  of  the  United  States

he
“The  Senate  of  tue  United  States  shall  be composed  of  two

_____It  is  admitted  that  petitioner  had  the  requisite  qualifications

179,  U.  S.  58,  62,  that

Ole

.

“The  right  to  vote  for  members  of  the Congress  of  the
United  States  is not  derived  merely  from  the  Constitution  and

|

What,  then,  is  meant  when  the  above  quoted  constitutional  pro-
visions  say  that  United  States  Senators  and  Representatives  shall  be
elected  “by the  people”  of the  several  States?

Under  the  familiar  rule,  of  course,  resort  must  be  had  “to  sub-
stance,  not  to  form;”  and  the  controlling  principle  is  that  laid
down  by this  Court  in  Ex Parte  Yurbrough,  supra  (p. 666),  where
it is said:

r

“It  is  as essential  to  the  successful  working  of this  govern
ment  that  the  great  organisms  of  its  executive  and  legislative
branches  should  be  the  free  choice  of the  people,  as  that  the
original  form  of  it  should  be.”  (Italics  ours).

_

Can.it be said that the members of Congress chosen in  the cles
tion  involved  in  this  case  were  “the  free  choice  of  the  people”  of
Texas?

That  petitioner  had no  real  part  in the choice  is beyond  questiom

(a)  In  Ex  Parte  Yarbrough,  supra,  (p.  665)  it is said:

States  may  not discriminate against  this  new  constitutional  right
created:  by  the  15th  Amendment.  “A gtate may  not  discriminate
against  rights  arising  under  federal laws.”  McKnett  v. St.  Louis
and  San  Francisco  Railway  Co.,  292  U.  S. 230,.234.

Petitioner  believes  it to  be  clear  that the Staté  of  Texas  has  dis
criminated  against  petitioner's  “new  constitutional  right”  created
by the  15th  Amendment.  One  instance  of  such  discrimination  by
the  State  of Texas  lies in  the following:

By Article  3107  of  the  Revised  Civil  Statutes  of  Texas,  1925,  as
amended,  the State  of  Texas  created  certain  state  exemptions  from
discrimination in  the exercise  of the elective  franchise  i in  the  election
involved  in  this  case,  as  follows:

“Provided  that no person  shall  ever  be denied the sgl to  par-
ticipate  ina  primary  in  this  state  because  of  former  political  |
views  or  affiliations  or  because  of  membership  or  non-mem-
bership  in  organizations  other  than  the  political  party.”

In  Love  v..  Wilcox,  28  S.  W.  (2d)  515,  the  Supreme  Court  of
Texas  held  that  these  state  exemptions  were  valid  and  enforceable  in
the  statutory  Democratic  primary  elections  of  the  State.  —

'

Petitioner  respectfully  submits  that  to  protect  the  state  exemptions
while  refusing  to  protect  the  Federal  exemption,  is a  discrimination
against  a  right  arising  under  the  15th  Amendment,  and  is  within
the  constitutional  prohibition  announced  by  this  Court  in  the
McKnett  Case,  supra.

This  discrimination  is  believed  by  petitioner  ‘to deny  him the
equal  protection  of the  laws  guaranteed  by the  14th  Amendment
to  the  Constitution  of  the  United  States,  in  that,  manifestly,  the
\«ssification  of race  and  color  is not  treated  by the  State  of Texas
with  the  same  equality  of  treatment  as  the  classifications  of  past
party  affiliations  and  membership  or  non-membership  in. organiza-
tions  other  than  the  political  party.

_(b)  Section  1 of  the  15th  Amendment  provides  as  follows:

“The right of citizens  of the United  States  to  vote shall not
be denied  or  abridged  by the United  States  or  by any State  on
account  of  race,  color  or  previous  condition  of  servitude.”

Guinn  v.  United  States  and  Myers  v.  Anderson,  supra,  establishéd

In  Bell  v.  Hill,  supra,  where  the  resolution  involved  in  this  case
was  before  it, the Supretne  Court of Texas  did ‘not question  the  con-
tention  that  the  convention  was  created  by  Article  3167  for  the
purpose  of  electing  delegates  to the  National  Democratic  Conven-
tion,  but  held  (pp.  121-122)  that  this  purpose  was  not  exclusive,
since  “There is no limitation  contained  in Article 3167  with  refer-
ence  to  declarations  of  policy  by  a  State  Democratic  Convention
called for the purpose of electing delegates to a  National Conven-

tion.”

This Court is not concluded,  of course,  by the decision of the
‘state court that a  convention  which is
ted by a statute  has both
statutory and non-statutory  powers,  if  such decision/ of the state
court has “no fair  support.”

Ancient  Egyptian  Arabic  Order  of  Nobles  of  the  Mystic

_  “An  attentive  examination  of  the  record  discloses  not  only

port  in the  evidence,  but  that the evidence  conclusively  refutes

tions  of law  and  one  question of fact inhering  in  and  necessary  to.
a  decision  of the Federal  questions  f law.  These  three  propositions
appear  from  the  following  three  quotations  from  Nixon  v. Con-
don,  supra:

(1)  “Whatever  our  couclusion  might  be if the statute  had  re-

qualifications  of  its  members,  nothing  of  the  kind  was  done,’  .
Instead,  the statute  lodged  the  power  in  a  committee,  which

the law.”  (p. 84, Italics  ours).

(2)  “The pith of the matter is simply this, that, then those
agencies  are invested  with  an authority  independent  of  the
ee  ee  eee
they  become  to  that extent  the organs  of the State itsel/,  the
repositories  of official  power.  They are then  the governmental
instruments  whereby  parties  are organized  and regulated  to the
end that government  itself  may be established  or  coritinued.
,  What they do in that relation, they must do in submission  to
the mandates  of equality an® liberty that bind officials  every-
where.”  (p. 88, Italics  ours).

(3)  “Whether

in given  circumstances  parties  or  their  com-
mitiees  are agencies  of  government  within  the Fourteenth  or
the Fifteenth  Amendment  is  a  question  which this court will
determine
for itself.  It is not concluded  upon  such  an inquiry
by decisions  rendered  elsewhere.  The test is not whether  the
members  of the executive  committee  are the representatives
of the State in the strict sense in which  an agent is  the repre-
sentative  of his principal.  The test is whether  they  are to  be
classified  as representatives  of the State to such an  extent  and
in such a sense  that  the  great  restraints of the  Constitution

“get  limits to their action.”  (p.  88,  Italics  ours).*

(a):  The Democratic  Party  is a  national  political  party;  it is not

A  few quotations from Bryce’s The Ainerican Commonweg)th will

show  beyond  question  this historical  truth.

:
“What  constitutes  a  party?  In  America,  there  is a  simple  test.  .
Any section  of men who nominate  candidates  of their own for

Commonwealth,

Vol. 2, p. 41. (Italics  ours.)

“We might therefore expect that the State parties would be
as distinct from the national parties pe are the State Covers
ments from the Federal.

“The contrary has happened.  The national  parties  have é-
gulfed the State parties. The latter have disappeared absolutely
as independent  bodies, and survive merely as branches of the
national parties, workinig each in. ite own State for the tenets
and purposes  which  a  national  party professes  and seeks to
attain.”  (Id.,  Vol.  1, p.  572. Italics ours).

“Accordingly,  the national parties have complete possession
of the field. In every State from Maine to Texas, all  State elec-
tions for the governorship  and other offices  are fought  om
their  lines;  all State legislatures  are divided  into  members
belonging to one or  other of them. Every trial of strength  in «
State election  is assumed  to presage  a  similar  result  in  «  na-
tional election.  Every State office  is deemed  as fitting  a  re
ward for services  to the national  party as for services in State
contests. In fact, the whole machinery  is worked exactly as if
the State were merely a  subdivision  of the Union for electoral
purposes.”  (Id., p.  574,  Italics ours.)
(b)  The Democratic Party being «  national political party, ite

supreme  voice  is the National  Democratic  Convention.

In Nixon v. Condon,  supra, this Court said the following  (p. 84) :
“Whatever  inherent  power a  State political  party has to de
termine the content of its membership resides in the State Con-
vention,  (Sayen, Modern  Democracies,  vol. 2, p. 40.)”  (Italics
nar

On page 46 of the same authority (Bryce, Modern Democracis,
Vol. 2),  writing to the same point with reference to national  politi-
cal parties,
it is said:

fn  the United ‘States the National Convention  is the su-
preme  exponent  of party  doctrine  and policy,  universally
recognized  as the party oracle.”

Whatever  inherent  power the Democratic  Party has, therefore,

to  determine  the  content  of  its  membership  resides,  not  in  forty-
eight  separate  and  distinc:  and  different  State  Democratic  conven-
tions,  not even in the State  Democratic  Convention  of  Texas,  as
would  be  true  if  it were  a  state  political  party—but  that  inherent
power,  such  as  exists  in  America  today,  resides  in  the  National

Any  power  which  the  State  Democratic  Convention  of  Texas
may  claim  to determine  the content  of  the membership  of  the
Démocratic  Party  must,  therefore,  necessarily  come  to  it  as  a
matter  of  delegation  from  the  Natjonal  Democratic  Convention.

‘

Petitioner  alleged  in  his  petition  below  that  no  power  to  exclude
:  Negroes  had  been  thus delegated  (R.  11).  The  general  demurrer
_  (R.  13),  of  course,  concedes  this  fact.  -

Not  only  has  the  National  Democratic  Convention  not  delegated
any  such  power,  but  it closed  its  platform  adopted  at Chicago  on
June  27,  1932,  (subsequent  to  May 24,  1932,  the  date  of the  resolu-
tion  of  the  Texas  State  Democratic  Convention  confining  member-
ship  in  the  Democratic  Party  to  white  people)  with these  words:

_“To  accomplish  these  purposes  and  to  recover  economic
liberty  we  pledge  the  nominees  of  this  convention  and  the
best  effort  of  a  great  party  whose  founder  announced  the  |
doctrine  which  guides  us  now,  in  the  hour  of  our  country’s
need  ‘equal  rights  to  all,  special  privileges  to  none.’””  (World  -
Almanac,  1933,  p.  933.  Italics  ours.)

ae

It  is  a  matter  of  historical  and  general  knowledge,  too,  that
Negroes  were  seated  in the  National  Democratic  Convention  of 1932.
It  is  also.  significant  that  the  Texas  State  Democratic  Convention
which  sought  by  the  resolution  involved  in  this  case  to confine
membership in the  Democratic  Party  to  the  white  race,  was  meeting
pursuant  to  a  Texas  statute,  Article  3167  of  the  Revised  Civil
Statutes  of  Texas,  for  the  express  and  only  purpose  of  electing
_  delegates  to  this  same  National  Democratic  Convention  of 1932.
The Supreme  Gourt of Texas states, in Bell v.  Hill,  supra,  at page 114,  .
that  such  was the nature  and  purpose  of the State  Democratic
Convention,  which  adopted  the  resolution  involved  in  this  case:

_  “The petition  states in substance,  in part, that the respondents
are  denying  or  will  deny,  the  relators  the  right to  vote,  because
of  a  resolution  passed  by the  State  Convention  of  the  Demo-,

“This  resolution  was  passed by the  Democratic  Convention
which  met  at  Houston  for  the  purpose  of  electing  delegates
to  the  National  Convention,  of the  Democratic  Party.”
(c)  The  Texas  State  Democratic  Convention  had  no  inherent
power  by virtue  of its being  to  say  who  may  be Democrats  and  who
may  not.
“The  National  Convention  is  the  supreme  exponent  of  party
doctrine  and  policy.”  Bryce,  Modern  Democracies,  supra.  As  ex-
plained  in  Bryce’s  The  American  Commonwealth,  supra,  the  State
convention  in  Texas  is  merely  a  branch  or  subdivision  of  the
national  party,  and. as such  is vested  only  with  the  management  of
the  party’s  affairs  in  a  single  state.  Except  when  duly  authorized
by the  supreme  voice  of  the  Democratic  Party,  which  it  was  not
in  this  case,  it  has  ‘no  power  to  exercise  the  supreme  inherent
power  of the  Democratic  Party.  The  reasons  were  plainly  stated  by
this  Court  in  Nixon  v.  Condon,  supra  (p. 86),  as  follows:
“The management  of the affairs  of a group  already  associated
together  as  members  of  a  party  is  obviously  a  very  different
function  from  that  of  determining  who  the  members  of  the
group  shall  be.  If  another  view  were  to  be  accepted,  a  com-
mittee might  rule out  of the  party  a  faction  distasteful  to  itself,
and  exclude  the  very  men  who  had  helped  to  bring  it  into

;

|

”  existence.”
Founded  by Thomas  Jefferson  long  prior even  to  the  Indepen-
dence  of  Texas  from  Mexico,  to  say  nothing  of its  statehood,  the
members  of the  Democratic  Party  were  already associated  together
as  a  party  for  several  decades  prior  to  the  creation  of  the State  -
Democratic  Convention  of TexasIt  follows  then  that  this  state
convention  could  not,  ipso  facto,  and  by  its  own  ipse  dixit,  and
merely  by  receiving  the  privilege  of  itself  becoming  a  branch  of
-  the  Democratic  Party,  suddenly  ascend  into  possession  by virtue  of
its  being  of  all  of  the  inherent  powers  of  the  party,  with  full
authority  to  rule  out even  those who  let the Texas  State  Democratic  |
Convention  into  the  Demtocratic  Party.

(d)  The Texas  State  Democratic  Convention  having  neither
delegated  nor inherent  power  to make  race and color  a  test for
membership  in the Democratic  Party,  it is inescapable  that what-
ever power it has in this regard  has come to it by virtue of Sections
2 and 27 of the Bill of Rights  (Article  1) of the Texas Constitution,
va...  Hill, 74
S. W.  (2d)  113.

In  Nixon v. Canton, ‘engets; tide Cane tabi Gaus tho Seat Dame

eratic  Executive  Committee  was an agency  of the state  in  depriving
Negroes  of their vote because  that committee  had “excluded  peti-
tioner  and others  of his race,  not by virtue  of any  authority
delegated  by the party,  but by virtue  of an authority  originating
or supposed  to originate  in the mandate  of the law.”

;

The same  is true of the Texas  State  Democratic  Convention  in
the case at bar, and that convention  is likewise,  for the same reason,
an agency
power,  the
of the state.  Speaking  of the convention’s
Supreme  Court of Texas  said at page  122 of Bell v.  Hill, supra;

“We are clearly  of the opinion  that the resolution  passed
by the Democratic  State Convention  at Houston  was a  valid
resolution  under  the power  clearly  guaranteed  to that body
by the Bill of Rights  of this State.”  (Italics  ours).

In  Nixon v. Condon,  supra,  this Court said of the same  power
Claimed by the State Democratic Executive Committes, the following
(p.  85):

“If  the State had not conferred  it, there would be hardly

color of right  to give a  basis for its exercise.”
Likewise,  when  the  State  Democratic  Convention

of  Texas
_  arrogates  unto itself this same power without  authority  from the
@upreme  voice  of the Democratic  Party,  petitioner  respectfully
submits  that “if the State had not” guaranteed  “it, there would
be hardly  color of right to give a  basis  for its exercise.”

(e)  This Court is not bound by decisions  of the Supreme  Court
and Courts  of Civil Appeals  of Texas holding  that the Texas State
Democratic  Convention  is not an agency  of government.

In Nixon v.  Condon,  supra, this Court said the following  (p. 88) :
“Whether  in given circumstances  parties  or their  committees
are agencies
of government  within  the  Fourteenth  or  the
Fifteenth  Amendment  is a  question  which  this  court  will

4l

determine  for itself.  /t is not concluded  upon such an inquiry
rendered  elsewhere.”  (Italics  ours.)  —
by decisions

Not only is this ultimate  Federal  question  open for review by
this Court,  but this Court may also decide  for itself every other
question which inheres in or is necessary  to a  proper decision of
that Federal  question.  Such an incidental  question is the question
‘whether  the Texas State Democratic  Convention,  as a  branch of the
Democratic  Party,  has the inherent  power by virtue of its being to
make race and color a  test of being a  Democrat,  in the very teeth
of a  subsequent  declarat’ on of the National  Democratic  Convention—
which  is said by Bryce  to be “the  supreme  exponent  of party  ©
doctrine  and policy”—dedicating
the Democratic  Party to a policy
|
of “equal rights to all, special privileges  to none.”
The general  principle  of this Court’s  power and duty to decide
for itself such questions  has been recently  restated  by this Court
through  Mr. Chief Justice  Hughes in Sterling  v.  Constantin,  287
WU. S. 378, 397, as follows:

. |

the exercise  of State  power would be but impotent  phrases,
the futility of which the State may at any time disclose  by the
simple  process  of trarisferring  powers of legislation  to the
E
Governor  to be  exercised  by him,  beyond  control,  upon his

has been decided  in @ great variety  of circumstances  that. when
questions  of law and fact are so intermingled  as to miake is
necessary  in order to pass upon the federal question,  the court
may, and should,  analyze  the facts.  Even when the case comes

42

to  this  Court  from  a  State  Court  this  duty  must  be  performed
as  a  necessary  incident  to  a  decision  upon  the  claim  of denial
of Federal  right.”  (Italics  ours.)

In  the  more  recent  case  of  Johnson  Oil  Refining  Company  v.

Oklahoma,  290  U.  S.  158,  159,  this  Court  said:

_

“The  Supreme  Court  of  the  State  reached  the  conclusion
that  all  the  cars  had  their  ‘taxable  situs’  at  the  latter  place.
As  the  asserted  Federal  right  turns  upon  the  determination  of
the  question  of  situs,  it  is  our  province  to  analyze  the  facts.
in  order  to  apply  the  law,  and  thus  to  ascertain  whether  the
conclusion  of  the  state court  has  adequate  iafans in  the
evidence.”

Besides,  the  question  involved  is  a  question  of  general  law  and
jurisprudence,  and  as  to  such  questions  this  Court  has  always
reserved  the  right  to  exercise  its  own  independent  judgment.

Swift  v.  Tyson,  10  L.  Ed.  865.

Burgess v. Seligman,  107  U. S. 20.

Sim  v.  Edenborn,  242  U.  S, 131.

.

See  also:  Barclay  v.  Hayes,  208  Fed.  319.

Point Number  Seven

Séctions  2 and  27  of  the  Bill  of  Rights  (Art.  1)  of  the
Constitution:  of  the  State  of  Texas,  as  construed  by  the
Supreme  Court  of  Texas  in  Bell  v.  Hill,  74  S.  W.-(2d)  113,
and  as  sustained  by the  judgment  below  in  the  case  at  bar,
are  unconstitutional  for  the  reason  that  they  deny  to  the
classification  of  race  and  color  the  benefits  which  are
guaranteed  to  the  classifications  of  past  party  affiliations
and =  or  non-membership  in  organizations  other
than  political  parties  by  Article  3107  of  the  Revised  Civil
Statutes  of  Texas,  as  upheld  by  the  Supreme  Court  of
Texas  in  the  case  of Love  v.  Wilcox,  28  S.  W.  (2d)  515.

The  denial  by the  judgment  of  the  court  below.  of this  claim  of
Federal  right  is not  in  accord  with  such  decisions  of this Court  as
Travis  v.  Yale  & Towne  Mfg.  Co.,  252  U.  $. 60,  and  Connolly  v.
’  Union  Sewer  Pipe Co.,  184  U. S. 540,  holding  that  where  one  class
is  favored  by exemptions  denied  to  ‘another  class  under  the  state
law,  there  must  be some  reasonable  basis  for  the  classifrcation  thus

established  in  order  for  it  to meet the requirements  of the equal
protection  clause  of  the  Fourteenth  Amendment.

That  race and color cannot  be made the basis of such a  classifi-  ©

cation

has been well settled  by this Court:
.  “States  may do a  good deal of classifying  that it is difficult
to  believe Fational,  but  there  are  limits,  and  it is too  clear  for
extended  argument  that  color  cannot  be  made  the  basis .of a
statutory  classification  affecting  the  right  set  up  in  this  case.”

?

Nixon  v.  Herndon,  2733  U.  S.  536,  541.

It  should  be kept in mind  also under this point  that we are not
dealing  with  a  convention  formed  at the instance  or  suggestion  of
private  individuals  upon  their  own  yolition.  But,  on  the  contrary,
Judge  Kennerly
in White v. The
Committee,  supra,  “the  convention

-  as said  by  United  States
County  Democratic  E

+  itself is  a  creation  of a statute  which  fixes the time of its meeting,  -
‘the’ purpose  of its meeting,  and prescribes  in detail  the manner  of .
choosing  those  who  participate  therein.  Article  3167,  Texas  Revised
Civil  Statutes  1925.”  Moreover,  as *  said by the  Supreme  Court  of
Texas in  Sterling  v.  Ferguson,  53  S. W.  (2d)  753,  760,  the  election
involved  is a  “statutory  primary  election.”

And it  is in  such @  statutory  election  and to  such a statutory  crea-
ture  that  Sections  2 and  27  of  the  Texas  Bill  of  Rights  guarantee
the  power  to  make  race  and  color  a  qualification  and  test  of voting,
while  Article  3107  of  the  Revised  Civil  Statutes,  held  valid  by the
28,.5..W.  (2d)  515,
Texas  Supreme  Court  in  Love  v.  Wilcox,
.  exempts  from  such  discrimination  the  classifications  of  past  party
affiliations  (the  traditional  test  of  party  loyalty)  and  membership
or  non-membership  in  organizations  other  than  the  political  party.
privileges  of men  whose  own

Now,  on  this Ist day of February,  A. D. 1928, at a  regular  term
of this count’tame 6n regularly to be heard the above styled and
numberg¥“canse.  The  plaintiff,  L.  A.  Nixon,  appeared  in  person
and By counsel-and  announced  ready  for  trial,  and the defendants,
C.“C.  Herndon  and  Chas.  Porras,  having  been  duly  and  legally
served with notice, appeared not, but wholly  made default. Wiere-
upon came  the jury,  composed  of Jake Kaufman  and eleven other
good and lawful  men, who were impanelled  and sworn to try the
issues in said cause, and after having heard the pleadings read,
and? the evidence  submitted  by the  plaintiff,  and  the argument.  of’
counsel, the court instructed  the jury to find the issues in fav@r
of the  plaintiff,  and  the  jury  returned  the following  verdict:  “We,
the jury under instructions of the court, find for  the 'plaintiff  L. A.
Nixon, against the defendants, C. C. Herndon and Chas. Porras, in
the  sum  of  one  dollar.  Jake  Kaufman,  Foreman.”

‘
And in  accordance  therewith  the Court doth find: That the law
and the evidence is with  the  plaintiff;  that the plaintiff  was denied
the right to vote at the Primary  Election on July 26, 1924, for’ candi-
dates for office upon the Democratic ticket, by the defendants, and
that said refusal was a  denial to the plaintiff of his rights, and
that plaintiff  is damaged,  and thé Court  finds his ‘measure  of
damages
to. be $1.00.

(Signed)  Charles
A. Boynton, Judge.

This  cause  coming  on to be heard  on this  the 15th  day  of
.  October,  A. D.  1932, in open court,  the plaintiff  being present  in
person  and by his attorneys,  Fred  C. Knollenberg  and  E.  F.
Cameron,  and the defendants  being  present  by their attorney,  Ben
R. Howell,  and «  jury  being  waived,  all matters  of fact  as well
as of law was submitted  to the court;  and the court  having  heard
the pleadings  read, and being  fully  advised  in the premises  doth
find that the law and the facts are with the plaintiff  who is entitled
been

SUPREME  COURT OF THE UNITED  STATES.

No.  563.—OcropER  Term,  1934.

R.  R.  Grovey,  Petitioner,
vs.
i
Albert Townsend.

e
_  ) On  Writ  of  Certiorari  to  the
Justice  Court,  Precinct  No.  1,
Harris  County,  Texas.

[ April'1,.  1935.]

Mr.  Justice  Roserts  delivered  the  opinion  of the  Court.

The  petitidifer,  by  complaint  filed  in  the  Justice  Court  of  Har-
ris  County,  Texas,  alleged  that  although  he.is  a  citizen  of  the
United  States  and  of the  State  and County,  and  a  member  of and
believer  in  the tenets of the  Democratic  party, the  respondent,  the
county  elerk,  a  state  officer,  having  as  such  only  public  functions
to  perform,  refused  him  a  ballot  for  a  Democratic  party  primary
election,  because  he  is  of  the negro  race.  He  demanded  ten  dol-
lars  damages.
The  pleading  quotes  articles  of  the  Revised  Civil
Statutes  of  Texas  which  require  the  nomination  of  candidates  at
primary  elections  by:any  organized  political  party  whose  nomi-
nees  received  one  hundred  thousand  votes  or  more.at  the  preced-
ing general  election,  and  recites  that  agreeably  to  these  enactments
a Democratic  primary  election  was  held  on  July  28, 1934,  at which
petitioner  had  the  right  to  vote.
Referring  to  statutes  which
regulate  absentee  voting  at primary  elections,  the  complaint  states
the  petitioner  expected  to  be absent  from  the  county  on  the  date  of
the  primaary  election,  and  demanfled  of the  respondent  an  absentee
ballot,  which  was  refused  him  in virtue  of a resolution  of the state
Democratic  convention  of  Texas,  adopted  May  24,  1932,  which  is:
**Be  it  resolved,  that  all  white  citizens  of  the  State  of  Texas
who  are  qualified  to  vote  under  the  Constitution  and  laws  of  the
state  shall  be  eligible  to  membership  in  the  Democratic  party  and
as  such  entitled  to participate  in  its  deliberations.’’

The  gomplaint  charges  that  the  respondent  acted  without  legal
excuse  and  his  wrongful  and  unlawful  acts  constituted  a  violation
of  the  Fourteenth  and  Fifteenth  Amendments  of  the federal  con-
stitution.

2

Grovey ve. Townsend.

A  demurrer,  assigning  as reasons  that  the complaint  was in-
sufficient  in law and stated  no cause of action,  was sustained  ; and a
motion  for a  new trial,  reasserting  violation  of the federal  rights
mentioned  in the complaint,  was overruled.  We  granted  certior-
ari,  because  of the importance  of the federal  question  presented,
_  whieh  has not been  determined  by this  court."  Our jurisdiction is
clear,  as the Justice  Court is the highest  state court in which  a  de-
cision  may be had,’ and the validity  of the constitution  and stat-
tutes of the state  was drawn  in question  on the ground  of their being  —
repugnant  to the Constitution  of the United States.‘

The charge  is that respondent,  a  state  officer,  in refusing  to
furnish  petitioner  a  ballot,  obeyed  the law of -Texas,  and the con-
sequent  denial  of petitioner's  right  to vote  in the primary  election
becausd  of his race and color  was state  action  forbidden  by the fed-
eral constitution;  and  it  is claimed  that  former  decisions  require
us so to hold.  The cited cases are, however, not in point.  In Nizon
v.  Herndon,  273 U.  8.  536, a  statute  which  enacted  that  ‘‘in  no
*  event  shall a  negro  be eligible  to.participate  in a  Democratic  party
primary  election  held in the State  of Texas,’’  was pronounced  offen-
sive to the Fourteenth  Amendment.  In Nizon  v.  Condon,  286 U. 8.
73,  a  statute  was  drawn  in question  which  provided  that  ‘‘every
political  party in this State through  its State Executive  Committee
shal]  have  the power  to prescribe  the qualifications  of its own  mem-
bers and shall in its own way defermine  who shall be qualified  to
vote  or otherwise  participate  in such  political  party.’’  We  held
this  was a  delegation  of state  power  to the state  executive  commit-
tee and made  its determination  conclusive  irrespective  of any ex-
pression  of the party's  will  by  its convention,  and therefore  the
committee's  action  barring  negroes  from  the party  primaries  was
state  action  prohibited  by the Fourteenth  Amendment.  Here  the
qualifications  of citizens  to participate  in party  counsels  and. to
vote  at party  primaries  have  been  declared  by the representatives
of the party  in convention  assembled,  and this action  upon  its face

is not state  action.  The question  whether  under  the constitution
and  laws  of Texas  such  a  declaration  as to party  membership
amounts  to state  action  was expressly  reserved  in Nizon  v.  Con-
don, supra,
pp. 84-85.  Petitioner  insists  that  for various  reasons
the resolution  of the state convention  limiting  membership  in the  —
Democratic  party  in Texas  to white  voters  does not relieve  the ex-
clusion  of negroes  from participation  in Democratic  primary  elec-
tions of its true nature  as the act of the state.

First.  An  argument’pressed  upon  us in Nizon  v.  Condon,  supra,
which  we found  it  unnecessary  to consider,  is again presented.  It
is that  the primary  election  was held  under  statutory  compulsion  ;
is wholly  statutory  in origin  and incidents;  those charged  with
its management  have  been  deprived  by statute  and judicial  de-
cision  of  all power  to establish  qualifications  for participation
therein  inconsistent  with those laid  down  by the laws  of the state,
save only  that  the managers  of  such elections  have  been given  the
power  to deny  negroes  the vote.  It is further  urged  that while
the election  is designated  that of the Democratic  party,  the statutes
not only require  this method  of  selecting  party nominees,  but
define  the powers  and duties  of the party’s  representatives  and of
those who are to conduct  the election  so completely,  and make them
-  $0 thoroughly  officers  of  the state,  that any action  taken  by them
in connection  with the qualifications  of members  of the party is.
in  fact state action  and not party  action.
vised Civil Statutes of  Texas of  1925,' which by Article 2960, re- >!  !
quires  that  any party  whose  members  cast  more  than  one hundred
thousand  ballots  at  the previous  election,  shall  nominate  candidates
through  primaries,  and fixes the date at whieh they are to be held;
by Article  2939 requires  primary  election officials  to be qualified
voters;  by Article  2955 declares  the same  qualifications  for vot-
ing in such af election  as in  the general  elections;  by Article’
‘2956 permits  absentee  voting  as in a  general  election;  by Article
2978 requires  that only an official  ballot shall be used, as in a  gen-
eral election  ; by Articles  2980-2981  specifies the form of ballot and
how  it shall be marked,  as other sections  do for general  elections:
by Article  2984 fixes the number of ballots to be provided,  as another

In  support  of this view petitioner  refers  to Title 50 of  the Re-

“article  does for  general  elections;  by Articles  2986,  2987  and  2990
permits  the  use  of  voting  booths,  guard  rails,  and  ballot  boxes
which  by  other  statutes  are  provided  for  general  elections;  by  .
Articles  2998  and  3104  requires  the  officials  of  primary  elections
to  take  the  same  oath  as  officials  at the  general  elections;  by Article  °
3002  defines  the  powers  of judges  at  primary  elections;  by Articles
3003-3025  provides  elaborately  for  the  purity  of  the  ballot  box;
by Article  3028  commands  that  the  sealed  ballot  boxes  be delivered
to  the  county  clerk  after  the  election,  as  is  provided  by  another
article  for  the  general  election;  and  by Article  3041  confers  juris-
diction  of  election  contests  upon  district  courts,  as  is  done  by
another  article  with  respect  to  general  elections.  A  perusal  of
these  provisions  so  it  is said,  will  convince  that  the  state  has  pre-
_-seribed  and  regulated  party  primaries  as  fully  as  general  elections,

and  has  made  those  who  manage  the  primaries  state  officers  sub-  .
ject  to state  direction  and  control.

While  it is true  that  Texas  has  by its  laws  elaborately  provided
for  the  expression  of, party preferénce  as  to nominees,  has  required
that  preference  to  be  expressed  in  a  certain  form  of  voting,  and
has  attempted  in  minute  detail  to  protect  the  suffrage  of the  mem-
bers  of the  organization  against  fraud,  it is equally  true  that  the
primary  is  a  party  primary  ;‘the  expenses  of  it  are  not  borne  by
the  state,  but  by members  of the  party  seeking  nomination  (Arts.
3108 ; 3116)  ; the  ballots  are  furnished  not  by the  state,  but  by the
agencies  of  the  party  (Arts.  3109;  3119);  the  votes  are  counted
and  the  returns  made  by  instrumentalities  created  by  the  party
(Arts,  3123;  3124-5;  3127)  ; and  the  state  recognizes  the. state  con-
vention  as  the  organ  of  the  party  for  the  declaration  of  principles
and  the  formulation  of p@icies  (Arts.  3136 ; 3139).

We  are  told  that  in  Lage  v.  Wilcoz,  119  Texas  256,  the  Supreme
Court  of  Texas  held  the  state  was  within  its province  in  prohibit-
ing a  party  from  establishing  past  party  affiliations  or. membershi
in  non-political  organizations  as qualifications  or  tests  for  partici-
pation  in  primary  elections,  and  in  consequence  issued  its  writ  of
'  mandamus  against  the  members  of  the  state  executive  committee
of  the  Democratic  party  on  the  ground  that  they  were  public
functionaries.  fulfilling  duties  imposed ¢ on  them  by  law.
But  in
that  case  it was  said  (p. 272) :

‘‘We  are not  called  upon  to  determine  whether  a political  party
has  power,  beyond  statutory  control,  to prescribe  what  persons  shall

—

Grovey  vs. Townsend.

5

participate  as  voters  or  candidates  in  its conventions  or  primaries.
We  have  no such  state of facts  before  us.’’

After  referring

to Article  3107, which limits  the power of the
state  executive  committee  of  a  party  to  determine  who  shall  be
qualified  to vote  at  primary  elections,  the court said:

'

‘‘The  Committee’s  diseretionary  power  is  further  restricted  by
the  statute  directing  that  a  single,  uniform  pledge  be  required  of
the  primary  participants.  The  effect  of  the  stateles  is to  decline
to  give  recognition  to the lodgment  of  power  in a  State  Executive
Committee,  to  be exercised  at  its discretion.’

Although  it did  not  pass  upon  the  constitutianality  of  Section
3107,  as  we  did  in  Nizon  v.  Condon,  supra,  the  Court  thus  recog-
nized  the fact  upon  whieh  our decision  turhed,  that the effort was
to  vest  in  the  state  executive  committee  the  power  to  bind  the
party  by its  decision  as  to  who  might  be admitted  to membership.
In Bell v. Hill, 74 8. W. (2d)  113, the same  court,  in a  manda-
mus proceeding  instituted  after the adoption  by the state conven-
tion ‘of  the  resolution  of  May  24,  1932,  restricting  eligibility  for .

'

_  membership  in the  Democratic  party  to white  persons,  held  the
resolution  valid  and  effective.
After  a  full  consideration  of  the  °
nature  of political  parties  in the United  States,  the court concluded
that suchpurties  in  the state of Texas arise from the exercise  of the
free  will-and  liberty  of the citizens  composing  them; that they are  -
voluntary  associations  for political  action,  and are not  the creatures
of the state;  and further  decided  that Sections  2 and 27 of Article
1 of  the  State  Constitution  guaranteed  to citizens  the  liberty  of
'  forming  political  associations,  and the only limitation  upon this.
right  to be found  in that instrument  is  the clause which requires
the  maintenance  of a republican  form  of government.  The statutes
regulating  the  nomination  of candidates  by primaries  were  related
by the court  to the police  power,  but were held  not to extend  to
the  denial  of the right  of citizens  to  form  a  political  party  and  to  |
determine  who  might  associate  with  them  as  members  thereof.  The
court  declared  that  a proper  view  of the election  laws  of Texas,  and
their  history,  required  the  conclusion  that the  Democratic  party  in
that  state is.a  voluntary  political  association  and,  by  its  repre-
sentatives  assembled  in  convention,  has  the  power  to  determine
who  shall  be  eligible  for  membership  and,  as  such,  eligible  to par-
ticipate  in the  party’s  primaries.

_

Fourth.  The complaint states that candidates  for the offices of
_  Senator  and Representative  in Congress  were to be nominated  at
the primary  election  of  July  9,  1934, and that in Texas nomina-
tion by the Democratic  party is equivalent  to  election.  These facts
(the truth of which the demurrer  assumes)  the petitioner insists,”
without  more;  make  out  a  forbidden  discrimination.
A  similar
situation  may exist in other states  where one or another  party  in-
¢hades a  great  majority  of  the qualified  electors.  The argument is
that as a  negro  may not be denied  a  ballot  at a  general  election
on account  of  his race or color,  if exclusion  from the primary
renders  his vote at the general  election  insignificant  and useless,
the result  is to deny him the suffrage  altogether.  So to say is to
confuse  the privilege  of membership-in  a  party with the right to
vote for  one who is to hold  a  public  office.  With  the former the
state  need  have  no  concern;  with the latter  it  is bound  to concern
itself,  for  the general  election  is a function  of the  state  government

as

°

Grovey’ vs. Townsend.
8
and discrimination  by the state as respects‘participation  by negroes
on account of their race or color is prohibited  by the federal con-
stitution.

Pifth.  The complaint charges that the Demoeratie party has
never declared  a  purpose  to exclude  negroes.  The premise  upon:
which this conclusion  rests is that the party is not s  state body
but a  national  organization,  whose representative  is the national
so it is gaid, has re-
Democratic  convention.  No  such convention,
solved  to exclude  negroes  from membership.  We  have no occasion
to determine  the correctness  of the position,  since even if true it
does not tend to prove that the petitioner  was discriminated  against
or denied  any right to vote by the State of Texas. -Indeed
the con-
tention contradicts  any such conclusion,  for it assumes  merely that
a  state convention,  the representative  and agent of a  state associa-
tien, has usurped  the rightful  authority  of a  national  convention
which represents  a  larger  and superior  country-wide  association.

We find no ground  for holding  that the respondent  has in obedi-
ence to the mandate  of the law of Texas discriminated  against  the
petitioner or denied him any right guaranteed by the Fourteenth
ond Fifteenth Amendutents.
